India (04): (KL, KA)", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=5815473", "date": "2018-05-24 16:38:15", "main_text": "NIPAH VIRUS - INDIA (04): (KERALA, KARNATAKA)\n**********************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\nhttp://www.promedmail.org\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\nhttp://www.isid.org\n\n[1]\nDate: Thu 24 May 2018\nSource: The Pioneer [edited]\nhttp://www.dailypioneer.com/nation/nipah-feared-in-karnataka.html\n\n\nThe Nipah virus that has claimed over 13 lives in Kerala in the last few days seems to have spread to the neighbouring Karnataka; 2 suspected cases of Nipah virus infection have been reported from Mangaluru and Kasargod in Karnataka.\n\nThe outbreak is likely to hit tourism industry in the State as well, as Bahrain has issued a travel advisory asking its citizens to avoid visiting the Southern States while the UAE [United Arab Emirates] to urging its citizens in India to exercise caution and abide by the instructions of the local authorities. The Piniyari Government too has asked travellers to avoid visiting 4 northern districts Kozhikode, Malappuram, Wayanad and Kannur, in Kerala, even though the Union Health Ministry has said that the Nipah virus outbreak in Kerala is a localised occurrence and there is no threat of it spreading.\n\nAccording to reports, one patient is a 20-year-old woman from Kasargod and the other patient is a 75-year-old man from Mangaluru in Karnataka. It is suspected that the woman probably could have been exposed to the virus when she had visited the Perambra hospital in Kozhikode district of Kerala where a nurse was undergoing treatment for Nipah virus infection. The woman had been in close contact with the nurse. The nurse succumbed to the infection on [Mon 21 May 2018]. However, the 75-year-old man has no history of travel.\n\nSamples from both the patients have been sent to the Manipal Centre for Virus Research (MCVR), Manipal. The samples will be sent to the National Institute of Virology (NIV), Pune if the results from MCVR turn positive for Nipah Virus.\n\nTamil Nadu, Karnataka and Puducherry Governments have said that there is no need to panic in these States, but officials have been asked to be on alert in case of any suspected outbreak. Meanwhile, the central team has devised draft guidelines, case definitions, advisory for healthcare workers, information to the general public, advisories for sample collection and transportation and a brief on Nipah virus disease.\n\nIn an advisory issued by Kerala Health Secretary Rajeev Sadanandan, it was stated that travelling to any part of Kerala was safe. But if travellers wished to be extra cautious, they may avoid the 4 districts -- Kozhikode, Malappuram, Wayanad and Kannur, it said.\n\nThe UAE too has urged its citizens in India to exercise caution and abide by the instructions of Indian authorities following the outbreak of the Nipah virus in Kerala while Bahrain has issued a travel advisory. It has asked its nationals to avoid travelling to Kerala until an outbreak of the rare Nipah virus is under control, the Gulf state's consulate in Mumbai said on its Twitter account on [Wed 23 May 2018].\n\nThe virus can cause flu-like symptoms and brain damage, and the outbreak has already killed 10 people in southern India, with at least 9 more being treated, officials said.\n\nThere is no vaccine for the Nipah virus, carried by fruit bats and spread [among people] through contact with bodily fluids, the World Health Organisation (WHO) says. Treatment for the virus, which has a mortality rate of about 70 percent, is supportive care.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail Rapporteur Joseph Dudley\n\n[Appearance of cases in adjoining states is not surprising. Infected people can move across state lines, as can infected fruit bats. It is prudent for health care providers in neighboring states to be on the alert. - Mod.TY\n\nMaps of India: http://www.mapsofindia.com/maps/india/india-political-map.htm and http://healthmap.org/promed/p/308]\n\n******\n[2]\nDate: Thu 24 May 2018\nSource: NDTV, Press Trust of India (PTI) [edited]\nhttps://www.ndtv.com/kerala-news/kerala-government-issues-advisory-against-travelling-to-4-northern-districts-1856621\n\n\nWith 10 deaths due to the deadly Nipah virus being reported so far from northern Kerala, the state government yesterday [Wed 23 May 2018] issued an advisory asking travellers to avoid visiting the 4 districts of Kozhikode, Malappuram, Waynad and Kannur. \"Travelling to any part of Kerala is safe. However, if travellers wish to be extra cautious, they may avoid the 4 districts\", Health Secretary Rajeev Sadanandan said.\n\nThe government has also called for an all-party meeting at Kozhikode on [Fri 25 May [2018] to discuss the issue. MPs, MLAs, other representatives of people and leaders of various political parties would attend the meeting, Health minister K K Shylaja said.\n\nIn Kozhikode and Malappuram districts, 10 people have lost their lives to Nipah so far and 19 are undergoing treatment in various hospitals, including one in Waynad. Of the 13 confirmed cases, 10 people have died.\n\nIsolation wards have also been set up in Thalassery government hospital in Kannur, according to media reports.\n\nIn Kozhikode, from where 7 deaths have been reported, District Collector U V Jose has ordered temporary stoppage of all training programmes and summer camps in affected areas such as Changaroth, Koorachund, Kottur, Cheruvannur, Chekyad, Chakkittapara and Olavanna.\n\nThe anganwadis [a center that provides basic health care in Indian villages] in these regions too have been asked to close down to avoid the spread of the virus among children.\n\nHowever, no decision has been taken yet concerning the reopening of schools after the summer vacation. The administration is hoping that the situation will be under control by the time schools reopen after a week. \"The number of newly infected cases is very low now\", Mr. Jose said in a statement.\n\nIn neighbouring Malappuram, where 3 persons have died due to the virus, orders have been issued in 4 panchayats [basic units of local administration] to stop Anganwadi classes for the time being.\n\nA crisis management group has been constituted to coordinate the response of government agencies following the deaths in Kozhikode and Malappuram districts. The group comprises Additional Chief Secretaries of Health, Revenue, Labour, Home; Principal Secretary Forest, Director Animal Husbandry and state Epidemiologists, among others.\n\nKeeping in mind the sacrifice of the 28-year-old nurse at Perambra Taluk hospital, who died after being infected by the virus while treating her patients, the Government today decided to give a job to her husband and financial assistance of rs 10 lakh [USD 1465] each for her 2 sons -- aged 5 and 2. The next of kin of 9 others who died of the virus would be given an assistance of Rs. 5 lakh [USD 738] each.\n\nMeanwhile, DGP Loknath Behara warned that stern action would be taken against those who spread false information with regard to the virus in the social media. Creation of fake or false messages, spreading them to cause panic or public disorder are criminal acts and liable for investigation and prosecution. Directions have been issued to DGP (crimes) to register criminal cases against the creators of the messages and propagators, he said.\n\nAn expert team from the National Centre for Disease Control (NCDC), including its director, Dr. Sujeet Kumar Singh and Head of Epidemiology, Dr. S K Jain, and a high-level team from the All India Institute of Medical Sciences (AIIMS) are already in Kerala to take stock of the situation.\n\nSurveillance has been increased in all districts.\n\nThe outbreak of the virus infection, which is a newly emerging zoonosis that causes severe disease in both animals and humans, is suspected to be from an unused well which was infested with bats.\n\nThe natural host of the virus is believed to be fruit bats of the Pteropodidae family, _Pteropus_ genus.\n\nThe Kerala Health minister also said there was no need to be afraid of bats and their habitats should not to destroyed.\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail Rapporteur Joseph Dudley\n\n[A previous ProMED-mail post reported that bats roosing in a well may have been the source of Nipah virus infections in Kozhikode district's Changaroth village (see Nipah virus - India (03): (KL) conf. 20180522.5812214). The death rate\nof victims of the Nipah virus is very high, he said, as around 75 per\ncent of the infected people do not survive. Mahmudur urged the people\nnot to panic as the disease is now almost under control and they are\nvigilantly monitoring the situation.\n\nA total of 152 people were infected with the Nipah virus across the\ncountry since 2001, of whom 113 died, said IEDCR officials. The\nvirologists advise people in the Nipah-prevalent areas not to eat\nfruits that have been bitten by fruit bats, or drink the raw juices of\npalm and date trees. Heat kills the Nipah virus, said experts, so if\nthe juices are boiled they can do no harm to consumers. They also\nsuggested that date juice should not be kept in open pitchers.\n\nAccording to experts, most of the Nipah virus infection cases last\nyear [2010] were reported from January to May. This is the breeding\nperiod of fruit bats and pregnant bats harbour this virus and probably\ntransmit it to humans. When children collect fruits and eat them\nwithout washing, the virus discharged from bats' saliva is transmitted\nto them. Experts also urged the parents in Nipah-prone areas not to\nallow their children to play under fruit trees.\n\nMahmudur stressed the need for creating awareness of the virus among\nthe country's people, especially villagers. At the same time\ngovernment should set up a separate unit and introduce protective\nmeasures for treating Nipah virus victims in the hospitals, he said.\n\nIn 2004 a doctor and in 2010 an intern doctor, after being infected\nby the patients at the hospitals, were killed by Nipah virus\n[infection].\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[There were cases of Nipah virus infection in this same area a year\nago (January 2010; see ProMED-mail archive number 20100122.0250] . In\naddition to boiling palm juice, local villagers can construct bamboo\nskirts to deny fruit bat access to palm juice collection pots. It\nwould be of interest to know what measures have been taken to bring\ntransmission under control, other than the information provided to the\nvillagers.\n\nMod.CP provided a good summary of Nipah virus transmission and\nepidemiology in the ProMED-mail archive mentioned above. Unlike the\ninitial Nipah outbreak in Malaysia, human cases in Bangladesh do not\ninvolve pigs, transmission being from _Pteropus_ fruit bats to humans\nvia contaminated fruit or palm juice.\n\nAn image of a _Pteropus_ fruit bat can be found at\n<http://rpmedia.ask.com/ts?u=/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Pteropus_giganteus_fg01.JPG/180px-Pteropus_giganteus_fg01.JPG>.\n\nThe location of Faridpur and Rajbari districts (Dhaka division) in\ncentral Bangladesh, can be seen on the map at\n<http://www.miraebd.net/demo/Bangladesh Maps.htm>. The death rate\nof victims of the Nipah virus is very high, he said, as around 75 per\ncent of the infected people do not survive. Mahmudur urged the people\nnot to panic as the disease is now almost under control and they are\nvigilantly monitoring the situation.\n\nA total of 152 people were infected with the Nipah virus across the\ncountry since 2001, of whom 113 died, said IEDCR officials. The\nvirologists advise people in the Nipah-prevalent areas not to eat\nfruits that have been bitten by fruit bats, or drink the raw juices of\npalm and date trees. Heat kills the Nipah virus, said experts, so if\nthe juices are boiled they can do no harm to consumers. They also\nsuggested that date juice should not be kept in open pitchers.\n\nAccording to experts, most of the Nipah virus infection cases last\nyear [2010] were reported from January to May. This is the breeding\nperiod of fruit bats and pregnant bats harbour this virus and probably\ntransmit it to humans. When children collect fruits and eat them\nwithout washing, the virus discharged from bats' saliva is transmitted\nto them. Experts also urged the parents in Nipah-prone areas not to\nallow their children to play under fruit trees.\n\nMahmudur stressed the need for creating awareness of the virus among\nthe country's people, especially villagers. At the same time\ngovernment should set up a separate unit and introduce protective\nmeasures for treating Nipah virus victims in the hospitals, he said.\n\nIn 2004 a doctor and in 2010 an intern doctor, after being infected\nby the patients at the hospitals, were killed by Nipah virus\n[infection].\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[There were cases of Nipah virus infection in this same area a year\nago (January 2010; see ProMED-mail archive number 20100122.0250] . In\naddition to boiling palm juice, local villagers can construct bamboo\nskirts to deny fruit bat access to palm juice collection pots. It\nwould be of interest to know what measures have been taken to bring\ntransmission under control, other than the information provided to the\nvillagers.\n\nMod.CP provided a good summary of Nipah virus transmission and\nepidemiology in the ProMED-mail archive mentioned above. Unlike the\ninitial Nipah outbreak in Malaysia, human cases in Bangladesh do not\ninvolve pigs, transmission being from _Pteropus_ fruit bats to humans\nvia contaminated fruit or palm juice.\n\nAn image of a _Pteropus_ fruit bat can be found at\n<http://rpmedia.ask.com/ts?u=/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Pteropus_giganteus_fg01.JPG/180px-Pteropus_giganteus_fg01.JPG>.\n\nThe location of Faridpur and Rajbari districts (Dhaka division) in\ncentral Bangladesh, can be seen on the map at\n<http://www.miraebd.net/demo/Bangladesh Maps.htm>. The death rate\nof victims of the Nipah virus is very high, he said, as around 75 per\ncent of the infected people do not survive. Mahmudur urged the people\nnot to panic as the disease is now almost under control and they are\nvigilantly monitoring the situation.\n\nA total of 152 people were infected with the Nipah virus across the\ncountry since 2001, of whom 113 died, said IEDCR officials. The\nvirologists advise people in the Nipah-prevalent areas not to eat\nfruits that have been bitten by fruit bats, or drink the raw juices of\npalm and date trees. Heat kills the Nipah virus, said experts, so if\nthe juices are boiled they can do no harm to consumers. They also\nsuggested that date juice should not be kept in open pitchers.\n\nAccording to experts, most of the Nipah virus infection cases last\nyear [2010] were reported from January to May. This is the breeding\nperiod of fruit bats and pregnant bats harbour this virus and probably\ntransmit it to humans. When children collect fruits and eat them\nwithout washing, the virus discharged from bats' saliva is transmitted\nto them. Experts also urged the parents in Nipah-prone areas not to\nallow their children to play under fruit trees.\n\nMahmudur stressed the need for creating awareness of the virus among\nthe country's people, especially villagers. At the same time\ngovernment should set up a separate unit and introduce protective\nmeasures for treating Nipah virus victims in the hospitals, he said.\n\nIn 2004 a doctor and in 2010 an intern doctor, after being infected\nby the patients at the hospitals, were killed by Nipah virus\n[infection].\n\n--\nCommunicated by:\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\n\n[There were cases of Nipah virus infection in this same area a year\nago (January 2010; see ProMED-mail archive number 20100122.0250] . In\naddition to boiling palm juice, local villagers can construct bamboo\nskirts to deny fruit bat access to palm juice collection pots. It\nwould be of interest to know what measures have been taken to bring\ntransmission under control, other than the information provided to the\nvillagers.\n\nMod.CP provided a good summary of Nipah virus transmission and\nepidemiology in the ProMED-mail archive mentioned above. Unlike the\ninitial Nipah outbreak in Malaysia, human cases in Bangladesh do not\ninvolve pigs, transmission being from _Pteropus_ fruit bats to humans\nvia contaminated fruit or palm juice.\n\nAn image of a _Pteropus_ fruit bat can be found at\n<http://rpmedia.ask.com/ts?u=/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Pteropus_giganteus_fg01.JPG/180px-Pteropus_giganteus_fg01.JPG>.\n\nThe location of Faridpur and Rajbari districts (Dhaka division) in\ncentral Bangladesh, can be seen on the map at\n<http://www.miraebd.net/demo/Bangladesh Maps.htm>. The\nHealthMap/ProMED-mail interactive map of Bangladesh can be accessed at\n<http://healthmap.org/r/0rkS>. - Mod.TY]\nSee Also\n2010\n----\nNipah virus, fatal - Bangladesh: (FR) 20100122.0250\n2008\n----\nNipah virus - Bangladesh (05): transmission 20080410.1310\nNipah virus - Bangladesh (04): transmission 20080406.1263\nNipah virus, fatal - Bangladesh (03): (Dhaka) 20080311.0979\nNipah virus, fatal - Bangladesh (02): (Dhaka) 20080308.0953\nNipah virus, fatal - Bangladesh: (Dhaka) 20080303.0873\n2005\n----\nNipah virus - Bangladesh (Tangail) (02) 20050211.0468\n.................................................mpp/ty/mj/mpp\n*##########################################################*\n************************************************************\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. Their blood samples have been sent to the\nNational Institute of Virology, Pune, for confirmatory tests.\nOf the 30 patients, 20 are from Betai, close to the Bangladesh border,\nabout 150 km from Calcutta. Nadia chief medical officer Mrinal Kanti Biswas\nsaid the decision to procure ribavarin was taken following advice from\nexperts at the Pune institute. \"We are buying the drug from Hyderabad and\nwill distribute it free to those who show symptoms of the affliction or run\ntemperature for days,\" said Biswas.\nA 3 member medical board has been set up to monitor fever cases being\nreported to hospitals and health centres in the district. Suspected\npatients will be kept under observation in isolation wards. Besides a\ndistrict hospital in Krishnagar, Nadia has 4 subdivisional hospitals and 7\nblock hospitals.\nNipah has no cure, but ribavarin would alleviate the symptoms of nausea,\nvomiting, and convulsion, said doctors. The virus, which has claimed 3\nlives in the district and afflicted many, could have come from Bangladesh.\nTapas Majumder, the first victim from Betai, had been to the neighbouring\ncountry on a business trip in February. \"He was running a temperature\nwithin days of his return,\" district magistrate S Meena said.\nThe virus, 1st detected in Malaysia in 1999, has since been known to strike\nonly 2 other places, Bangladesh and Bengal. It killed 105 of the 265\npatients in Malaysia, 18 of 30 in Bangladesh in April 2004, and [75 per\ncent] of the 66 it infected in Siliguri in 2001.\n(byline: Rabi Banerjee)\n--\ncommunicated by:\nProMED-mail rapporteur Joseph P Dudley, PhD\n[Ribavirin (Virazole) is a nucleoside analog considered to have broad\nspectrum antiviral activity against several DNA- and RNA-containing\nviruses. It is relatively toxic and accordingly its use has been\nrestricted. Notably it has been used in life-threatening respiratory\nsyncytial virus infection in infancy, against certain exotic viruses such\nas Lassa fever virus and more recently Nipah virus, and in combination with\npegylated interferon against certain genotypes of hepatitis C virus.\nAlthough first synthesized in 1972 its node of antiviral activity is\nunclear. Among other effects, ribavirin monophosphate is a competitive\ninhibitor of cellular monophosphate dehydrogenase which results in\nreduction of GTP (guanosine triphosphate) pools in treated cells. The use\nof ribavirin for treatment of the symptoms of suspected Nipah virus\ninfection in patients in West Bengal with undefined fevers seems a rather\nextreme responds at this juncture. - Mod.CP]\nSee Also\nNipah virus, fatal - Bangladesh (05): transmission", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=71543", "date": "2007-05-11 19:00:02", "main_text": "NIPAH VIRUS, FATAL - INDIA (WEST BENGAL) (02)\n*********************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mailis a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\nDate: Thu 10 May 2007\nSource: The Telegraph, Calcutta, Thu 10 May 2007 [edited]\n<http://www.telegraphindia.com/1070510/asp/bengal/story_7757746.asp>\n\nRibavirin for treatment of suspected Nipah cases\n------------------------------------------------\nThe anti-viral drug ribavarin, which was widely used in Malaysia when Nipah\nstruck there in 1999, is being used in Nadia to treat the suspected cases.\nHealth officials here said ribavarin, a tablet of which costs around Rs 75\n[USD 1.84], will be given to the 30 patients down with fever at Betai\nvillage and in Krishnagar. Their blood samples have been sent to the\nNational Institute of Virology, Pune, for confirmatory tests.\nOf the 30 patients, 20 are from Betai, close to the Bangladesh border,\nabout 150 km from Calcutta. Nadia chief medical officer Mrinal Kanti Biswas\nsaid the decision to procure ribavarin was taken following advice from\nexperts at the Pune institute. \"We are buying the drug from Hyderabad and\nwill distribute it free to those who show symptoms of the affliction or run\ntemperature for days,\" said Biswas.\nA 3 member medical board has been set up to monitor fever cases being\nreported to hospitals and health centres in the district. Suspected\npatients will be kept under observation in isolation wards. Besides a\ndistrict hospital in Krishnagar, Nadia has 4 subdivisional hospitals and 7\nblock hospitals.\nNipah has no cure, but ribavarin would alleviate the symptoms of nausea,\nvomiting, and convulsion, said doctors. The virus, which has claimed 3\nlives in the district and afflicted many, could have come from Bangladesh.\nTapas Majumder, the first victim from Betai, had been to the neighbouring\ncountry on a business trip in February. \"He was running a temperature\nwithin days of his return,\" district magistrate S Meena said.\nThe virus, 1st detected in Malaysia in 1999, has since been known to strike\nonly 2 other places, Bangladesh and Bengal. It killed 105 of the 265\npatients in Malaysia, 18 of 30 in Bangladesh in April 2004, and [75 per\ncent] of the 66 it infected in Siliguri in 2001.\n(byline: Rabi Banerjee)\n--\ncommunicated by:\nProMED-mail rapporteur Joseph P Dudley, PhD\n[Ribavirin (Virazole) is a nucleoside analog considered to have broad\nspectrum antiviral activity against several DNA- and RNA-containing\nviruses. It is relatively toxic and accordingly its use has been\nrestricted. Notably it has been used in life-threatening respiratory\nsyncytial virus infection in infancy, against certain exotic viruses such\nas Lassa fever virus and more recently Nipah virus, and in combination with\npegylated interferon against certain genotypes of hepatitis C virus.\nAlthough first synthesized in 1972 its node of antiviral activity is\nunclear. Among other effects, ribavirin monophosphate is a competitive\ninhibitor of cellular monophosphate dehydrogenase which results in\nreduction of GTP (guanosine triphosphate) pools in treated cells. The use\nof ribavirin for treatment of the symptoms of suspected Nipah virus\ninfection in patients in West Bengal with undefined fevers seems a rather\nextreme responds at this juncture. - Mod.CP]\nSee Also\nNipah virus, fatal - Bangladesh (02): (Dhaka)", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=64712", "date": "2007-05-11 19:00:02", "main_text": "NIPAH VIRUS, FATAL - INDIA (WEST BENGAL) (02)\n*********************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mailis a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\nDate: Thu 10 May 2007\nSource: The Telegraph, Calcutta, Thu 10 May 2007 [edited]\n<http://www.telegraphindia.com/1070510/asp/bengal/story_7757746.asp>\n\nRibavirin for treatment of suspected Nipah cases\n------------------------------------------------\nThe anti-viral drug ribavarin, which was widely used in Malaysia when Nipah\nstruck there in 1999, is being used in Nadia to treat the suspected cases.\nHealth officials here said ribavarin, a tablet of which costs around Rs 75\n[USD 1.84], will be given to the 30 patients down with fever at Betai\nvillage and in Krishnagar. Their blood samples have been sent to the\nNational Institute of Virology, Pune, for confirmatory tests.\nOf the 30 patients, 20 are from Betai, close to the Bangladesh border,\nabout 150 km from Calcutta. Nadia chief medical officer Mrinal Kanti Biswas\nsaid the decision to procure ribavarin was taken following advice from\nexperts at the Pune institute. \"We are buying the drug from Hyderabad and\nwill distribute it free to those who show symptoms of the affliction or run\ntemperature for days,\" said Biswas.\nA 3 member medical board has been set up to monitor fever cases being\nreported to hospitals and health centres in the district. Suspected\npatients will be kept under observation in isolation wards. Besides a\ndistrict hospital in Krishnagar, Nadia has 4 subdivisional hospitals and 7\nblock hospitals.\nNipah has no cure, but ribavarin would alleviate the symptoms of nausea,\nvomiting, and convulsion, said doctors. The virus, which has claimed 3\nlives in the district and afflicted many, could have come from Bangladesh.\nTapas Majumder, the first victim from Betai, had been to the neighbouring\ncountry on a business trip in February. \"He was running a temperature\nwithin days of his return,\" district magistrate S Meena said.\nThe virus, 1st detected in Malaysia in 1999, has since been known to strike\nonly 2 other places, Bangladesh and Bengal. It killed 105 of the 265\npatients in Malaysia, 18 of 30 in Bangladesh in April 2004, and [75 per\ncent] of the 66 it infected in Siliguri in 2001.\n(byline: Rabi Banerjee)\n--\ncommunicated by:\nProMED-mail rapporteur Joseph P Dudley, PhD\n[Ribavirin (Virazole) is a nucleoside analog considered to have broad\nspectrum antiviral activity against several DNA- and RNA-containing\nviruses. It is relatively toxic and accordingly its use has been\nrestricted. Notably it has been used in life-threatening respiratory\nsyncytial virus infection in infancy, against certain exotic viruses such\nas Lassa fever virus and more recently Nipah virus, and in combination with\npegylated interferon against certain genotypes of hepatitis C virus.\nAlthough first synthesized in 1972 its node of antiviral activity is\nunclear. Among other effects, ribavirin monophosphate is a competitive\ninhibitor of cellular monophosphate dehydrogenase which results in\nreduction of GTP (guanosine triphosphate) pools in treated cells. The use\nof ribavirin for treatment of the symptoms of suspected Nipah virus\ninfection in patients in West Bengal with undefined fevers seems a rather\nextreme responds at this juncture. - Mod.CP]\nSee Also\nNipah virus, fatal - For assistance from a\nhuman being send mail to: owner-promed@promedmail.org.\n############################################################\n############################################################", "reports": [{"diseases": ["other", "hepatitis c", "lassa fever", "nipah virus", "encephalitis"], "syndromes": ["encephalitis"], "event_date": ["1999-03-16 00:00:00", "2021-02-16 00:00:00", "1999-03-16 00:00:00", "2004-04-16 00:00:00", "2001-03-16 00:00:00", "1972-03-16 00:00:00", "2021-03-02 00:00:00", "2021-03-02 00:00:00", "2004-03-16 00:00:00", "2004-03-16 00:00:00", "2004-03-16 00:00:00", "2021-03-02 00:00:00", "2004-03-16 00:00:00", "2021-03-02 00:00:00", "2021-03-04 00:00:00", "2003-03-16 00:00:00", "2001-03-16 00:00:00", "2002-03-16 00:00:00", "2001-03-16 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "Malaysia", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Faridpur", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Krishnagar", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "FATAL", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Betai", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "India", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Bengal", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Nadia", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "West Bengal", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Bangladesh", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "North Bengal", "country": "unknown"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "10201", "headline": "PRO/AH/EDR> Nipah virus, fatal - India (West Bengal)", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=10201", "date": "2004-04-23 23:50:00", "main_text": "NIPAH VIRUS - BANGLADESH (2004): WHO WER\n***********************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n\nDate: Fri 23 Apr 2004\nFrom: Alfonso Rodriguez <arodriguezm@SaludFMV.org>\nSource: World Health Organization (WHO), Weekly Epidemiological Record, No.\n17, 2004, 79, 161-172, Fri 23 Apr 2004 [abbreviated and edited]\n<http://www.who.int/wer/2004/wer7917/en/>\n\nBangladesh: Nipah Virus Outbreaks from Jan to Apr 2004\n----------------------------------------------\nOn 17 Jan 2004, a 9-year-old child from Kazipara village was brought to the\nemergency room of Goalanda health complex in Rajbari district, Bangladesh.\nThe patient had a 3-day history of fever and worsening neurological signs.\nHe died within 15 minutes of admission. Between 17 and 21 Jan 2004, 6\nadditional children (aged between 2 and 15 years) and a 28-year-old woman\n(mother of the youngest child) also died with the same symptoms. All\npatients came from the neighboring Kazipara and Juran Molla Para villages\nand lived in 2 groups of houses located within 1 km of each other. On 21\nJan 2004, Goalanda health authorities triggered the alert and sent a report\nto the central level. On 22 Jan 2004, a joint investigation mission with\nthe Institute of Epidemiology Disease Control and Research (IEDCR) of the\nMinistry of Health, the Centre for Health and Population Research (ICDDRB),\nand the WHO country office was dispatched to Goalanda to verify the rumors\nand to collect samples for laboratory diagnosis.\nBetween 22 and 27 Jan 2004, the investigation team was able to confirm the\noutbreak and to provide a 1st description of the epidemic. Blood samples\nobtained from several patients were sent to the Special Pathogen Branch\n(SPB) at the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC) in Atlanta,\nUSA. On 4 Feb 2004, SPB-CDC detected IgM antibodies against Nipah virus in\nthe serum of 5 patients. The laboratory investigations included ELISA tests\nfor IgG and IgM antibodies, reverse transcriptase polymerase chain reaction\nto detect the viral genome, and viral isolation. CDC was able to culture\nthe virus from 2 of the patients. Viral RNA was amplified to show that a\nNipah virus -- with about a 95 percent homology with that isolated during\nthe Malaysian outbreak in 1999 -- was the cause of the outbreak.\nSurveillance\n------------\nIn light of the findings of previous epidemic investigations and of the\nclinical presentation of the first 8 confirmed Bangladeshi cases, 3\ncategories were employed for case definition: (1) suspect, (2) probable,\nand (3) laboratory-confirmed. In addition, when samples were collected from\nprobable and suspect cases, they were reclassified as \"non-cases\" if they\nwere negative for Nipah virus IgM antibodies. Routine epidemiological\nsurveillance was reinforced by an active suspect-encephalitis\ncase-notification system -- implemented in Faridpur, Manikgonj, and Rajbari\ndistricts -- surrounding the outbreak epicenter. Surveillance was also\nreinforced by IEDCR across the entire territory.\nEpidemiology\n------------\nDescriptive epidemiology was undertaken based on the case definition. A\ncase of Nipah virus infection was any probable or laboratory-confirmed\ncase. As at 23 Feb 2004, 23 Nipah cases -- including 11\nlaboratory-confirmed cases -- had been identified in 6 districts (Faridpur,\nGolpagonj, Manikgonj, Joypurat, Naogaon, Rajbari). 17 of the 23 cases died\n(case-fatality rate of 74 percent). In all the affected districts, cases\nwere clustered geographically and in time. The 1st identified case\ndeveloped the 1st symptoms of the disease on 11 Jan 2004. Most of the\ndetected cases occurred between 12 and 25 Jan 2004 (for 20 out of 23 cases)\nsuggesting a common source of contamination. [Figure 1 (not shown here)\nillustrates the weekly distribution of cases (survivors and deaths) of\nNipah virus infection in 6 districts of Bangladesh as of 23 Feb 2004. -\nMod.CP.]\nThe main focus, with 12 Nipah cases, was identified in Goalanda, Rajbari\ndistrict, where an in-depth investigation was undertaken. Of the 12 cases,\n9 were male (75 percent) and aged under 19 years. The investigation failed\nto establish any link between these cases and sick pigs -- or other types\nof sick mammals -- as was observed during the Malaysian Nipah outbreak.\nConversely, it was noted in Goalanda that young boys collected and ate\nfruits in the trees before dawn. This observation gave rise to the\nhypothesis that contamination could have occurred while eating the same\nfruits that fruit bats (_Pteropodidae_) fed on during the night.\nSeveral epidemiological studies were conducted by the investigation team in\nGoalanda. These included a case-control study to identify risk factors for\ninfection, a seroprevalence study to assess the spread of the infection\n(antibody detection) in the general population, and a follow-up of\nconvalescent cases to assess antibody kinetics and duration of viral\nshedding. Fieldwork has been completed, and data analysis has begun.\nClinical description\n---------------------\nMost of the identified cases were transferred to the Dhaka Medical College\nHospital and the Dhaka Shishu Hospital where definitive case management was\norganized. Among 11 patients with laboratory-confirmed Nipah virus\ninfection, the most commonly reported signs and symptoms included fever\n(11/11), headache (4/11), vomiting (5/11), dizziness (5/11), altered\nconsciousness or total or partial loss of consciousness (11/11), coma\n(9/11), focal neurological signs (3/11), disorientation/hallucination\n(4/11), seizure (8/11), respiratory distress (6/11) and cough/cold (3/11).\nEcological studies\n------------------\nEcological studies were conducted to establish the source of human\ninfection and identify the natural reservoir of Nipah virus in Bangladesh.\nThe team focused its work in the Goalanda area and sampled domestic,\nperidomestic and wild animals living in or around the affected villages. It\nprioritized taxa associated with previous Nipah virus outbreaks in Malaysia\n(i.e. Megachiropteran bats and pigs). Laboratory investigations are\ncurrently under way at SPB-CDC, although preliminary results confirm that\nseveral _Pteropus_ flying foxes have evidence of Nipah virus infection.\nSocial mobilization and health education\n------------------\nThe Ministry of Health and the WHO team relayed all relevant information to\nthe press on a regular basis thereby keeping the public regularly informed\nabout the evolution of the situation and of investigations. Given the\nresults of the epidemiological investigation, it was proposed that several\nhealth education messages be prepared to improve hygiene while eating\nfruits (washing, peeling, cooking fruits before eating them, and\nhand-washing at home) in order to break the primary infection and\ntransmission of the disease. The support of medical anthropologists and\nexperts in social mobilization will be needed to prepare these messages.\nCluster of Nipah Cases in Faridpur District, Mar - Apr 2004\n-----------------\nOn 19 Apr 2004, the Bangladesh Ministry of Health reported one cluster of\n30 Nipah cases, including 18 deaths from 13 Mar to 14 Apr in Faridpur\ndistrict. Laboratory testing, performed by CDC, confirmed Nipah virus\ninfection in 16 of the cases.\nThe outbreak was investigated by a team comprising experts from the IEDCR,\nICDDRB and the WHO country office. All cases lived in Guha Laksmipur\nvillage or were in contact with a spiritual leader living in this village\nwho was himself a case. Direct contact with ill patients is suspected to\nhave played a role in the transmission of the disease and the spread of\nthis outbreak.\nThe team organized infection-control and safe clinical-management workshops\nfor local medical staff in different health care settings of the Faridpur\ndistrict. Investigations are continuing.\nConclusion\n----------\nAfter Meherpur (2001) and Naogaon (2003), Bangladesh faces again an\nepidemic of Nipah. As in 2001 and 2003, few cases were identified, but this\ntime suspect cases are more widely spread throughout the country. Until\nstudies have been completed, and control and prevention measures discussed,\nthe investigation team has proposed the following short-term recommendations:\n1. Creation of a task force for Nipah outbreaks using a multidisciplinary\napproach.\n2. Creation of special funds for outbreak response and to support families\nand hospitals.\n3. Institution of urgent measures to prevent nosocomial infection in all\nhospitals of the country (patient isolation, injection safety, safe waste\ndisposal, etc.).\n4. Preparation of prevention messages recommending hand-washing after\ntaking care of sick relatives and washing fruit, and peeling or cooking it,\nbefore eating.\nRecommendations also included medium-term activities such as strengthening\noutbreak detection and investigation capacity, strengthening basic\nlaboratory support, transferring Nipah laboratory diagnosis capacity to\nBangladesh, and conducting further studies on the ecology of _Pteropodidae_\nin Bangladesh.\nAn international workshop focusing on case-management, and a scientific\nmeeting on Nipah virus and Hendra virus, are planned to be held in Dhaka in\n2004 in collaboration with experts from Australia, Malaysia, Thailand and\nUnited States.\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\nSee Also\nNipah/Hendra-like virus - Bangladesh (2001/2003) 20040202.0398\nNipah/Hendra - like virus - Bangladesh (Rajbari) 20040206.0431\nNipah virus - Bangladesh (Faridpur) confirmed 20040420.1102\nUnexplained deaths - Bangladesh (Faridpur)(02): Nipah susp 20040419.1087\nUnexplained deaths - Bangladesh (Faridpur): RFI 20040407.0941\nUndiagnosed deaths - Bangladesh (Rajbari):RFI 20040125.0292\nUndiagnosed deaths - Bangladesh (Rajbari)(02): RFI 20040129.0336\nUndiagnosed deaths - Bangladesh (Rajbari) (03) 20040130.0346\nUndiagnosed deaths - Bangladesh (Rajbari)(04): Nip... 20040201.0387\n2002\n----\nNipah-like virus - Bangladesh (2001) 20020830.5187\n2001\n----\nUndiagnosed disease - Bangladesh: alert 20010524.1006\nUndiagnosed disease - Bangladesh: RFI 20010512.0919\nUnexplained deaths - India (North Bengal): RFI 20010220.0328\nUnexplained deaths - India (North Bengal) (11) 20010330.0643\n....................cp/msp/pg/mpp\n\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. All\npatients came from the neighboring Kazipara and Juran Molla Para villages\nand lived in 2 groups of houses located within 1 km of each other. On 21\nJan 2004, Goalanda health authorities triggered the alert and sent a report\nto the central level. On 22 Jan 2004, a joint investigation mission with\nthe Institute of Epidemiology Disease Control and Research (IEDCR) of the\nMinistry of Health, the Centre for Health and Population Research (ICDDRB),\nand the WHO country office was dispatched to Goalanda to verify the rumors\nand to collect samples for laboratory diagnosis.\nBetween 22 and 27 Jan 2004, the investigation team was able to confirm the\noutbreak and to provide a 1st description of the epidemic. Blood samples\nobtained from several patients were sent to the Special Pathogen Branch\n(SPB) at the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC) in Atlanta,\nUSA. On 4 Feb 2004, SPB-CDC detected IgM antibodies against Nipah virus in\nthe serum of 5 patients. The laboratory investigations included ELISA tests\nfor IgG and IgM antibodies, reverse transcriptase polymerase chain reaction\nto detect the viral genome, and viral isolation. CDC was able to culture\nthe virus from 2 of the patients. Viral RNA was amplified to show that a\nNipah virus -- with about a 95 percent homology with that isolated during\nthe Malaysian outbreak in 1999 -- was the cause of the outbreak.\nSurveillance\n------------\nIn light of the findings of previous epidemic investigations and of the\nclinical presentation of the first 8 confirmed Bangladeshi cases, 3\ncategories were employed for case definition: (1) suspect, (2) probable,\nand (3) laboratory-confirmed. In addition, when samples were collected from\nprobable and suspect cases, they were reclassified as \"non-cases\" if they\nwere negative for Nipah virus IgM antibodies. Routine epidemiological\nsurveillance was reinforced by an active suspect-encephalitis\ncase-notification system -- implemented in Faridpur, Manikgonj, and Rajbari\ndistricts -- surrounding the outbreak epicenter. Surveillance was also\nreinforced by IEDCR across the entire territory.\nEpidemiology\n------------\nDescriptive epidemiology was undertaken based on the case definition. A\ncase of Nipah virus infection was any probable or laboratory-confirmed\ncase. As at 23 Feb 2004, 23 Nipah cases -- including 11\nlaboratory-confirmed cases -- had been identified in 6 districts (Faridpur,\nGolpagonj, Manikgonj, Joypurat, Naogaon, Rajbari). 17 of the 23 cases died\n(case-fatality rate of 74 percent). In all the affected districts, cases\nwere clustered geographically and in time. The 1st identified case\ndeveloped the 1st symptoms of the disease on 11 Jan 2004. Most of the\ndetected cases occurred between 12 and 25 Jan 2004 (for 20 out of 23 cases)\nsuggesting a common source of contamination. [Figure 1 (not shown here)\nillustrates the weekly distribution of cases (survivors and deaths) of\nNipah virus infection in 6 districts of Bangladesh as of 23 Feb 2004. -\nMod.CP.]\nThe main focus, with 12 Nipah cases, was identified in Goalanda, Rajbari\ndistrict, where an in-depth investigation was undertaken. Of the 12 cases,\n9 were male (75 percent) and aged under 19 years. The investigation failed\nto establish any link between these cases and sick pigs -- or other types\nof sick mammals -- as was observed during the Malaysian Nipah outbreak.\nConversely, it was noted in Goalanda that young boys collected and ate\nfruits in the trees before dawn. This observation gave rise to the\nhypothesis that contamination could have occurred while eating the same\nfruits that fruit bats (_Pteropodidae_) fed on during the night.\nSeveral epidemiological studies were conducted by the investigation team in\nGoalanda. These included a case-control study to identify risk factors for\ninfection, a seroprevalence study to assess the spread of the infection\n(antibody detection) in the general population, and a follow-up of\nconvalescent cases to assess antibody kinetics and duration of viral\nshedding. Fieldwork has been completed, and data analysis has begun.\nClinical description\n---------------------\nMost of the identified cases were transferred to the Dhaka Medical College\nHospital and the Dhaka Shishu Hospital where definitive case management was\norganized. Among 11 patients with laboratory-confirmed Nipah virus\ninfection, the most commonly reported signs and symptoms included fever\n(11/11), headache (4/11), vomiting (5/11), dizziness (5/11), altered\nconsciousness or total or partial loss of consciousness (11/11), coma\n(9/11), focal neurological signs (3/11), disorientation/hallucination\n(4/11), seizure (8/11), respiratory distress (6/11) and cough/cold (3/11).\nEcological studies\n------------------\nEcological studies were conducted to establish the source of human\ninfection and identify the natural reservoir of Nipah virus in Bangladesh.\nThe team focused its work in the Goalanda area and sampled domestic,\nperidomestic and wild animals living in or around the affected villages. It\nprioritized taxa associated with previous Nipah virus outbreaks in Malaysia\n(i.e. Megachiropteran bats and pigs). Laboratory investigations are\ncurrently under way at SPB-CDC, although preliminary results confirm that\nseveral _Pteropus_ flying foxes have evidence of Nipah virus infection.\nSocial mobilization and health education\n------------------\nThe Ministry of Health and the WHO team relayed all relevant information to\nthe press on a regular basis thereby keeping the public regularly informed\nabout the evolution of the situation and of investigations. Given the\nresults of the epidemiological investigation, it was proposed that several\nhealth education messages be prepared to improve hygiene while eating\nfruits (washing, peeling, cooking fruits before eating them, and\nhand-washing at home) in order to break the primary infection and\ntransmission of the disease. The support of medical anthropologists and\nexperts in social mobilization will be needed to prepare these messages.\nCluster of Nipah Cases in Faridpur District, Mar - Apr 2004\n-----------------\nOn 19 Apr 2004, the Bangladesh Ministry of Health reported one cluster of\n30 Nipah cases, including 18 deaths from 13 Mar to 14 Apr in Faridpur\ndistrict. Laboratory testing, performed by CDC, confirmed Nipah virus\ninfection in 16 of the cases.\nThe outbreak was investigated by a team comprising experts from the IEDCR,\nICDDRB and the WHO country office. All cases lived in Guha Laksmipur\nvillage or were in contact with a spiritual leader living in this village\nwho was himself a case. Direct contact with ill patients is suspected to\nhave played a role in the transmission of the disease and the spread of\nthis outbreak.\nThe team organized infection-control and safe clinical-management workshops\nfor local medical staff in different health care settings of the Faridpur\ndistrict. Investigations are continuing.\nConclusion\n----------\nAfter Meherpur (2001) and Naogaon (2003), Bangladesh faces again an\nepidemic of Nipah. As in 2001 and 2003, few cases were identified, but this\ntime suspect cases are more widely spread throughout the country. Until\nstudies have been completed, and control and prevention measures discussed,\nthe investigation team has proposed the following short-term recommendations:\n1. Creation of a task force for Nipah outbreaks using a multidisciplinary\napproach.\n2. Creation of special funds for outbreak response and to support families\nand hospitals.\n3. Institution of urgent measures to prevent nosocomial infection in all\nhospitals of the country (patient isolation, injection safety, safe waste\ndisposal, etc.).\n4. Preparation of prevention messages recommending hand-washing after\ntaking care of sick relatives and washing fruit, and peeling or cooking it,\nbefore eating.\nRecommendations also included medium-term activities such as strengthening\noutbreak detection and investigation capacity, strengthening basic\nlaboratory support, transferring Nipah laboratory diagnosis capacity to\nBangladesh, and conducting further studies on the ecology of _Pteropodidae_\nin Bangladesh.\nAn international workshop focusing on case-management, and a scientific\nmeeting on Nipah virus and Hendra virus, are planned to be held in Dhaka in\n2004 in collaboration with experts from Australia, Malaysia, Thailand and\nUnited States.\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\nSee Also\nNipah/Hendra-like virus - Bangladesh (2001/2003) 20040202.0398\nNipah/Hendra - like virus - Bangladesh (Rajbari) 20040206.0431\nNipah virus - Bangladesh (Faridpur) confirmed 20040420.1102\nUnexplained deaths - Bangladesh (Faridpur)(02): Nipah susp 20040419.1087\nUnexplained deaths - Bangladesh (Faridpur): RFI 20040407.0941\nUndiagnosed deaths - Bangladesh (Rajbari):RFI 20040125.0292\nUndiagnosed deaths - Bangladesh (Rajbari)(02): RFI 20040129.0336\nUndiagnosed deaths - Bangladesh (Rajbari) (03) 20040130.0346\nUndiagnosed deaths - Bangladesh (Rajbari)(04): Nip... 20040201.0387\n2002\n----\nNipah-like virus - Bangladesh (2001) 20020830.5187\n2001\n----\nUndiagnosed disease - Bangladesh: alert 20010524.1006\nUndiagnosed disease - Bangladesh: RFI 20010512.0919\nUnexplained deaths - India (North Bengal): RFI 20010220.0328\nUnexplained deaths - India (North Bengal) (11) 20010330.0643\n....................cp/msp/pg/mpp\n\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. Bangladesh (Faridpur) 2004: trans.", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=2207253", "date": "2004-04-23 23:50:00", "main_text": "NIPAH VIRUS - BANGLADESH (2004): WHO WER\n***********************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n\nDate: Fri 23 Apr 2004\nFrom: Alfonso Rodriguez <arodriguezm@SaludFMV.org>\nSource: World Health Organization (WHO), Weekly Epidemiological Record, No.\n17, 2004, 79, 161-172, Fri 23 Apr 2004 [abbreviated and edited]\n<http://www.who.int/wer/2004/wer7917/en/>\n\nBangladesh: Nipah Virus Outbreaks from Jan to Apr 2004\n----------------------------------------------\nOn 17 Jan 2004, a 9-year-old child from Kazipara village was brought to the\nemergency room of Goalanda health complex in Rajbari district, Bangladesh.\nThe patient had a 3-day history of fever and worsening neurological signs.\nHe died within 15 minutes of admission. Between 17 and 21 Jan 2004, 6\nadditional children (aged between 2 and 15 years) and a 28-year-old woman\n(mother of the youngest child) also died with the same symptoms. All\npatients came from the neighboring Kazipara and Juran Molla Para villages\nand lived in 2 groups of houses located within 1 km of each other. On 21\nJan 2004, Goalanda health authorities triggered the alert and sent a report\nto the central level. On 22 Jan 2004, a joint investigation mission with\nthe Institute of Epidemiology Disease Control and Research (IEDCR) of the\nMinistry of Health, the Centre for Health and Population Research (ICDDRB),\nand the WHO country office was dispatched to Goalanda to verify the rumors\nand to collect samples for laboratory diagnosis.\nBetween 22 and 27 Jan 2004, the investigation team was able to confirm the\noutbreak and to provide a 1st description of the epidemic. Blood samples\nobtained from several patients were sent to the Special Pathogen Branch\n(SPB) at the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC) in Atlanta,\nUSA. On 4 Feb 2004, SPB-CDC detected IgM antibodies against Nipah virus in\nthe serum of 5 patients. The laboratory investigations included ELISA tests\nfor IgG and IgM antibodies, reverse transcriptase polymerase chain reaction\nto detect the viral genome, and viral isolation. CDC was able to culture\nthe virus from 2 of the patients. Viral RNA was amplified to show that a\nNipah virus -- with about a 95 percent homology with that isolated during\nthe Malaysian outbreak in 1999 -- was the cause of the outbreak.\nSurveillance\n------------\nIn light of the findings of previous epidemic investigations and of the\nclinical presentation of the first 8 confirmed Bangladeshi cases, 3\ncategories were employed for case definition: (1) suspect, (2) probable,\nand (3) laboratory-confirmed. In addition, when samples were collected from\nprobable and suspect cases, they were reclassified as \"non-cases\" if they\nwere negative for Nipah virus IgM antibodies. Routine epidemiological\nsurveillance was reinforced by an active suspect-encephalitis\ncase-notification system -- implemented in Faridpur, Manikgonj, and Rajbari\ndistricts -- surrounding the outbreak epicenter. Fieldwork has been completed, and data analysis has begun.\nClinical description\n---------------------\nMost of the identified cases were transferred to the Dhaka Medical College\nHospital and the Dhaka Shishu Hospital where definitive case management was\norganized. Among 11 patients with laboratory-confirmed Nipah virus\ninfection, the most commonly reported signs and symptoms included fever\n(11/11), headache (4/11), vomiting (5/11), dizziness (5/11), altered\nconsciousness or total or partial loss of consciousness (11/11), coma\n(9/11), focal neurological signs (3/11), disorientation/hallucination\n(4/11), seizure (8/11), respiratory distress (6/11) and cough/cold (3/11).\nEcological studies\n------------------\nEcological studies were conducted to establish the source of human\ninfection and identify the natural reservoir of Nipah virus in Bangladesh.\nThe team focused its work in the Goalanda area and sampled domestic,\nperidomestic and wild animals living in or around the affected villages. It\nprioritized taxa associated with previous Nipah virus outbreaks in Malaysia\n(i.e. Megachiropteran bats and pigs). These included a case-control study to identify risk factors for\ninfection, a seroprevalence study to assess the spread of the infection\n(antibody detection) in the general population, and a follow-up of\nconvalescent cases to assess antibody kinetics and duration of viral\nshedding. Fieldwork has been completed, and data analysis has begun.\nClinical description\n---------------------\nMost of the identified cases were transferred to the Dhaka Medical College\nHospital and the Dhaka Shishu Hospital where definitive case management was\norganized. Among 11 patients with laboratory-confirmed Nipah virus\ninfection, the most commonly reported signs and symptoms included fever\n(11/11), headache (4/11), vomiting (5/11), dizziness (5/11), altered\nconsciousness or total or partial loss of consciousness (11/11), coma\n(9/11), focal neurological signs (3/11), disorientation/hallucination\n(4/11), seizure (8/11), respiratory distress (6/11) and cough/cold (3/11).\nEcological studies\n------------------\nEcological studies were conducted to establish the source of human\ninfection and identify the natural reservoir of Nipah virus in Bangladesh.\nThe team focused its work in the Goalanda area and sampled domestic,\nperidomestic and wild animals living in or around the affected villages. It\nprioritized taxa associated with previous Nipah virus outbreaks in Malaysia\n(i.e. Megachiropteran bats and pigs). Laboratory investigations are\ncurrently under way at SPB-CDC, although preliminary results confirm that\nseveral _Pteropus_ flying foxes have evidence of Nipah virus infection.\nSocial mobilization and health education\n------------------\nThe Ministry of Health and the WHO team relayed all relevant information to\nthe press on a regular basis thereby keeping the public regularly informed\nabout the evolution of the situation and of investigations. Given the\nresults of the epidemiological investigation, it was proposed that several\nhealth education messages be prepared to improve hygiene while eating\nfruits (washing, peeling, cooking fruits before eating them, and\nhand-washing at home) in order to break the primary infection and\ntransmission of the disease. The support of medical anthropologists and\nexperts in social mobilization will be needed to prepare these messages.\nCluster of Nipah Cases in Faridpur District, Mar - Apr 2004\n-----------------\nOn 19 Apr 2004, the Bangladesh Ministry of Health reported one cluster of\n30 Nipah cases, including 18 deaths from 13 Mar to 14 Apr in Faridpur\ndistrict. Laboratory testing, performed by CDC, confirmed Nipah virus\ninfection in 16 of the cases.\nThe outbreak was investigated by a team comprising experts from the IEDCR,\nICDDRB and the WHO country office. All cases lived in Guha Laksmipur\nvillage or were in contact with a spiritual leader living in this village\nwho was himself a case. Direct contact with ill patients is suspected to\nhave played a role in the transmission of the disease and the spread of\nthis outbreak.\nThe team organized infection-control and safe clinical-management workshops\nfor local medical staff in different health care settings of the Faridpur\ndistrict. Investigations are continuing.\nConclusion\n----------\nAfter Meherpur (2001) and Naogaon (2003), Bangladesh faces again an\nepidemic of Nipah. As in 2001 and 2003, few cases were identified, but this\ntime suspect cases are more widely spread throughout the country. Until\nstudies have been completed, and control and prevention measures discussed,\nthe investigation team has proposed the following short-term recommendations:\n1. Creation of a task force for Nipah outbreaks using a multidisciplinary\napproach.\n2. Creation of special funds for outbreak response and to support families\nand hospitals.\n3. Institution of urgent measures to prevent nosocomial infection in all\nhospitals of the country (patient isolation, injection safety, safe waste\ndisposal, etc.).\n4. Preparation of prevention messages recommending hand-washing after\ntaking care of sick relatives and washing fruit, and peeling or cooking it,\nbefore eating.\nRecommendations also included medium-term activities such as strengthening\noutbreak detection and investigation capacity, strengthening basic\nlaboratory support, transferring Nipah laboratory diagnosis capacity to\nBangladesh, and conducting further studies on the ecology of _Pteropodidae_\nin Bangladesh.\nAn international workshop focusing on case-management, and a scientific\nmeeting on Nipah virus and Hendra virus, are planned to be held in Dhaka in\n2004 in collaboration with experts from Australia, Malaysia, Thailand and\nUnited States.\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\nSee Also\nNipah/Hendra-like virus - Bangladesh (2001/2003) 20040202.0398\nNipah/Hendra - like virus - Bangladesh (Rajbari) 20040206.0431\nNipah virus - Bangladesh (Faridpur) confirmed 20040420.1102\nUnexplained deaths - Bangladesh (Faridpur)(02): Nipah susp 20040419.1087\nUnexplained deaths - Bangladesh (Faridpur): RFI 20040407.0941\nUndiagnosed deaths - Bangladesh (Rajbari):RFI 20040125.0292\nUndiagnosed deaths - Bangladesh (Rajbari)(02): RFI 20040129.0336\nUndiagnosed deaths - Bangladesh (Rajbari) (03) 20040130.0346\nUndiagnosed deaths - Bangladesh (Rajbari)(04): Nip... 20040201.0387\n2002\n----\nNipah-like virus - Bangladesh (2001) 20020830.5187\n2001\n----\nUndiagnosed disease - Bangladesh: alert 20010524.1006\nUndiagnosed disease - Bangladesh: RFI 20010512.0919\nUnexplained deaths - India (North Bengal): RFI 20010220.0328\nUnexplained deaths - India (North Bengal) (11) 20010330.0643\n....................cp/msp/pg/mpp\n\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. For assistance from a\nhuman being send mail to: owner-promed@promedmail.org.\n############################################################\n############################################################", "reports": [{"diseases": ["other", "nipah virus", "encephalitis"], "syndromes": ["encephalitis"], "event_date": ["2004-03-16 00:00:00", "1979-03-16 00:00:00", "2004-01-17 00:00:00", "2004-01-21 00:00:00", "2004-01-22 00:00:00", "2004-02-04 00:00:00", "1999-03-16 00:00:00", "2004-02-23 00:00:00", "2004-01-11 00:00:00", "2004-02-23 00:00:00", "2004-04-19 00:00:00", "2001-03-16 00:00:00", "2003-03-16 00:00:00", "2001-03-16 00:00:00", "2003-03-16 00:00:00", "2004-03-16 00:00:00", "2001-03-16 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "North Bengal", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Malaysia", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Faridpur", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Faridpur District", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Meherpur", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "India", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Kazipara", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Dhaka", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Australia", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "United States", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Rajbari district", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Thailand", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Atlanta", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Bangladesh", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Goalanda", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Naogaon", "country": "unknown"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "2205985", "headline": "PRO/AH> Nipah virus (2004) (03) - Bangladesh: background", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=2205985", "date": "2004-04-23 23:50:00", "main_text": "NIPAH VIRUS - BANGLADESH (2004): WHO WER\n***********************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n\nDate: Fri 23 Apr 2004\nFrom: Alfonso Rodriguez <arodriguezm@SaludFMV.org>\nSource: World Health Organization (WHO), Weekly Epidemiological Record, No.\n17, 2004, 79, 161-172, Fri 23 Apr 2004 [abbreviated and edited]\n<http://www.who.int/wer/2004/wer7917/en/>\n\nBangladesh: Nipah Virus Outbreaks from Jan to Apr 2004\n----------------------------------------------\nOn 17 Jan 2004, a 9-year-old child from Kazipara village was brought to the\nemergency room of Goalanda health complex in Rajbari district, Bangladesh.\nThe patient had a 3-day history of fever and worsening neurological signs.\nHe died within 15 minutes of admission. Between 17 and 21 Jan 2004, 6\nadditional children (aged between 2 and 15 years) and a 28-year-old woman\n(mother of the youngest child) also died with the same symptoms. All\npatients came from the neighboring Kazipara and Juran Molla Para villages\nand lived in 2 groups of houses located within 1 km of each other. On 21\nJan 2004, Goalanda health authorities triggered the alert and sent a report\nto the central level. On 22 Jan 2004, a joint investigation mission with\nthe Institute of Epidemiology Disease Control and Research (IEDCR) of the\nMinistry of Health, the Centre for Health and Population Research (ICDDRB),\nand the WHO country office was dispatched to Goalanda to verify the rumors\nand to collect samples for laboratory diagnosis.\nBetween 22 and 27 Jan 2004, the investigation team was able to confirm the\noutbreak and to provide a 1st description of the epidemic. Blood samples\nobtained from several patients were sent to the Special Pathogen Branch\n(SPB) at the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC) in Atlanta,\nUSA. On 4 Feb 2004, SPB-CDC detected IgM antibodies against Nipah virus in\nthe serum of 5 patients. The laboratory investigations included ELISA tests\nfor IgG and IgM antibodies, reverse transcriptase polymerase chain reaction\nto detect the viral genome, and viral isolation. CDC was able to culture\nthe virus from 2 of the patients. Viral RNA was amplified to show that a\nNipah virus -- with about a 95 percent homology with that isolated during\nthe Malaysian outbreak in 1999 -- was the cause of the outbreak.\nSurveillance\n------------\nIn light of the findings of previous epidemic investigations and of the\nclinical presentation of the first 8 confirmed Bangladeshi cases, 3\ncategories were employed for case definition: (1) suspect, (2) probable,\nand (3) laboratory-confirmed. In addition, when samples were collected from\nprobable and suspect cases, they were reclassified as \"non-cases\" if they\nwere negative for Nipah virus IgM antibodies. Routine epidemiological\nsurveillance was reinforced by an active suspect-encephalitis\ncase-notification system -- implemented in Faridpur, Manikgonj, and Rajbari\ndistricts -- surrounding the outbreak epicenter. Surveillance was also\nreinforced by IEDCR across the entire territory.\nEpidemiology\n------------\nDescriptive epidemiology was undertaken based on the case definition. A\ncase of Nipah virus infection was any probable or laboratory-confirmed\ncase. As at 23 Feb 2004, 23 Nipah cases -- including 11\nlaboratory-confirmed cases -- had been identified in 6 districts (Faridpur,\nGolpagonj, Manikgonj, Joypurat, Naogaon, Rajbari). 17 of the 23 cases died\n(case-fatality rate of 74 percent). In all the affected districts, cases\nwere clustered geographically and in time. The 1st identified case\ndeveloped the 1st symptoms of the disease on 11 Jan 2004. Most of the\ndetected cases occurred between 12 and 25 Jan 2004 (for 20 out of 23 cases)\nsuggesting a common source of contamination. [Figure 1 (not shown here)\nillustrates the weekly distribution of cases (survivors and deaths) of\nNipah virus infection in 6 districts of Bangladesh as of 23 Feb 2004. -\nMod.CP.]\nThe main focus, with 12 Nipah cases, was identified in Goalanda, Rajbari\ndistrict, where an in-depth investigation was undertaken. Of the 12 cases,\n9 were male (75 percent) and aged under 19 years. The investigation failed\nto establish any link between these cases and sick pigs -- or other types\nof sick mammals -- as was observed during the Malaysian Nipah outbreak.\nConversely, it was noted in Goalanda that young boys collected and ate\nfruits in the trees before dawn. This observation gave rise to the\nhypothesis that contamination could have occurred while eating the same\nfruits that fruit bats (_Pteropodidae_) fed on during the night.\nSeveral epidemiological studies were conducted by the investigation team in\nGoalanda. These included a case-control study to identify risk factors for\ninfection, a seroprevalence study to assess the spread of the infection\n(antibody detection) in the general population, and a follow-up of\nconvalescent cases to assess antibody kinetics and duration of viral\nshedding. Fieldwork has been completed, and data analysis has begun.\nClinical description\n---------------------\nMost of the identified cases were transferred to the Dhaka Medical College\nHospital and the Dhaka Shishu Hospital where definitive case management was\norganized. Among 11 patients with laboratory-confirmed Nipah virus\ninfection, the most commonly reported signs and symptoms included fever\n(11/11), headache (4/11), vomiting (5/11), dizziness (5/11), altered\nconsciousness or total or partial loss of consciousness (11/11), coma\n(9/11), focal neurological signs (3/11), disorientation/hallucination\n(4/11), seizure (8/11), respiratory distress (6/11) and cough/cold (3/11).\nEcological studies\n------------------\nEcological studies were conducted to establish the source of human\ninfection and identify the natural reservoir of Nipah virus in Bangladesh.\nThe team focused its work in the Goalanda area and sampled domestic,\nperidomestic and wild animals living in or around the affected villages. It\nprioritized taxa associated with previous Nipah virus outbreaks in Malaysia\n(i.e. Megachiropteran bats and pigs). Laboratory investigations are\ncurrently under way at SPB-CDC, although preliminary results confirm that\nseveral _Pteropus_ flying foxes have evidence of Nipah virus infection.\nSocial mobilization and health education\n------------------\nThe Ministry of Health and the WHO team relayed all relevant information to\nthe press on a regular basis thereby keeping the public regularly informed\nabout the evolution of the situation and of investigations. Given the\nresults of the epidemiological investigation, it was proposed that several\nhealth education messages be prepared to improve hygiene while eating\nfruits (washing, peeling, cooking fruits before eating them, and\nhand-washing at home) in order to break the primary infection and\ntransmission of the disease. The support of medical anthropologists and\nexperts in social mobilization will be needed to prepare these messages.\nCluster of Nipah Cases in Faridpur District, Mar - Apr 2004\n-----------------\nOn 19 Apr 2004, the Bangladesh Ministry of Health reported one cluster of\n30 Nipah cases, including 18 deaths from 13 Mar to 14 Apr in Faridpur\ndistrict. Laboratory testing, performed by CDC, confirmed Nipah virus\ninfection in 16 of the cases.\nThe outbreak was investigated by a team comprising experts from the IEDCR,\nICDDRB and the WHO country office. All cases lived in Guha Laksmipur\nvillage or were in contact with a spiritual leader living in this village\nwho was himself a case. Direct contact with ill patients is suspected to\nhave played a role in the transmission of the disease and the spread of\nthis outbreak.\nThe team organized infection-control and safe clinical-management workshops\nfor local medical staff in different health care settings of the Faridpur\ndistrict. Investigations are continuing.\nConclusion\n----------\nAfter Meherpur (2001) and Naogaon (2003), Bangladesh faces again an\nepidemic of Nipah. As in 2001 and 2003, few cases were identified, but this\ntime suspect cases are more widely spread throughout the country. Until\nstudies have been completed, and control and prevention measures discussed,\nthe investigation team has proposed the following short-term recommendations:\n1. Creation of a task force for Nipah outbreaks using a multidisciplinary\napproach.\n2. Creation of special funds for outbreak response and to support families\nand hospitals.\n3. Institution of urgent measures to prevent nosocomial infection in all\nhospitals of the country (patient isolation, injection safety, safe waste\ndisposal, etc.).\n4. Preparation of prevention messages recommending hand-washing after\ntaking care of sick relatives and washing fruit, and peeling or cooking it,\nbefore eating.\nRecommendations also included medium-term activities such as strengthening\noutbreak detection and investigation capacity, strengthening basic\nlaboratory support, transferring Nipah laboratory diagnosis capacity to\nBangladesh, and conducting further studies on the ecology of _Pteropodidae_\nin Bangladesh.\nAn international workshop focusing on case-management, and a scientific\nmeeting on Nipah virus and Hendra virus, are planned to be held in Dhaka in\n2004 in collaboration with experts from Australia, Malaysia, Thailand and\nUnited States.\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\nSee Also\nNipah/Hendra-like virus - Bangladesh (2001/2003) 20040202.0398\nNipah/Hendra - like virus - Bangladesh (Rajbari) 20040206.0431\nNipah virus - Bangladesh (Faridpur) confirmed 20040420.1102\nUnexplained deaths - Bangladesh (Faridpur)(02): Nipah susp 20040419.1087\nUnexplained deaths - Bangladesh (Faridpur): RFI 20040407.0941\nUndiagnosed deaths - Bangladesh (Rajbari):RFI 20040125.0292\nUndiagnosed deaths - Bangladesh (Rajbari)(02): RFI 20040129.0336\nUndiagnosed deaths - Bangladesh (Rajbari) (03) 20040130.0346\nUndiagnosed deaths - Bangladesh (Rajbari)(04): Nip... 20040201.0387\n2002\n----\nNipah-like virus - Bangladesh (2001) 20020830.5187\n2001\n----\nUndiagnosed disease - Bangladesh: alert 20010524.1006\nUndiagnosed disease - Bangladesh: RFI 20010512.0919\nUnexplained deaths - India (North Bengal): RFI 20010220.0328\nUnexplained deaths - India (North Bengal) (11) 20010330.0643\n....................cp/msp/pg/mpp\n\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. Preparation of prevention messages recommending hand-washing after\ntaking care of sick relatives and washing fruit, and peeling or cooking it,\nbefore eating.\nRecommendations also included medium-term activities such as strengthening\noutbreak detection and investigation capacity, strengthening basic\nlaboratory support, transferring Nipah laboratory diagnosis capacity to\nBangladesh, and conducting further studies on the ecology of _Pteropodidae_\nin Bangladesh.\nAn international workshop focusing on case-management, and a scientific\nmeeting on Nipah virus and Hendra virus, are planned to be held in Dhaka in\n2004 in collaboration with experts from Australia, Malaysia, Thailand and\nUnited States.\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\nSee Also\nNipah/Hendra-like virus - Bangladesh (2001/2003) 20040202.0398\nNipah/Hendra - like virus - Bangladesh (Rajbari) 20040206.0431\nNipah virus - Bangladesh (Faridpur) confirmed 20040420.1102\nUnexplained deaths - Bangladesh (Faridpur)(02): Nipah susp 20040419.1087\nUnexplained deaths - Bangladesh (Faridpur): RFI 20040407.0941\nUndiagnosed deaths - Bangladesh (Rajbari):RFI 20040125.0292\nUndiagnosed deaths - Bangladesh (Rajbari)(02): RFI 20040129.0336\nUndiagnosed deaths - Bangladesh (Rajbari) (03) 20040130.0346\nUndiagnosed deaths - Bangladesh (Rajbari)(04): Nip... 20040201.0387\n2002\n----\nNipah-like virus - Bangladesh (2001) 20020830.5187\n2001\n----\nUndiagnosed disease - Bangladesh: alert 20010524.1006\nUndiagnosed disease - Bangladesh: RFI 20010512.0919\nUnexplained deaths - India (North Bengal): RFI 20010220.0328\nUnexplained deaths - India (North Bengal) (11) 20010330.0643\n....................cp/msp/pg/mpp\n\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed.
