For assistance from a\nhuman being send mail to: owner-promed@promedmail.org.\n############################################################\n############################################################", "reports": [{"diseases": ["other", "nipah virus", "encephalitis"], "syndromes": ["encephalitis"], "event_date": ["2004-03-16 00:00:00", "1979-03-16 00:00:00", "2004-01-17 00:00:00", "2004-01-21 00:00:00", "2004-01-22 00:00:00", "2004-02-04 00:00:00", "1999-03-16 00:00:00", "2004-02-23 00:00:00", "2004-01-11 00:00:00", "2004-02-23 00:00:00", "2004-04-19 00:00:00", "2001-03-16 00:00:00", "2003-03-16 00:00:00", "2001-03-16 00:00:00", "2003-03-16 00:00:00", "2004-03-16 00:00:00", "2001-03-16 00:00:00"], "locations": [{"location": "North Bengal", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Malaysia", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Faridpur", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Faridpur District", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Meherpur", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "India", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Kazipara", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Dhaka", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Australia", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "United States", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Rajbari district", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Thailand", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Atlanta", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Bangladesh", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Goalanda", "country": "unknown"}, {"location": "Naogaon", "country": "unknown"}]}]}, {"archive_id": "2205630", "headline": "PRO/AH/EDR> Nipah virus - Bangladesh (2004): WHO WER", "url": "https://promedmail.org/promed-post/?id=2205630", "date": "2004-04-23 23:50:00", "main_text": "NIPAH VIRUS - BANGLADESH (2004): WHO WER\n***********************************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.promedmail.org>\nProMED-mail is a program of the\nInternational Society for Infectious Diseases\n<http://www.isid.org>\n\nDate: Fri 23 Apr 2004\nFrom: Alfonso Rodriguez <arodriguezm@SaludFMV.org>\nSource: World Health Organization (WHO), Weekly Epidemiological Record, No.\n17, 2004, 79, 161-172, Fri 23 Apr 2004 [abbreviated and edited]\n<http://www.who.int/wer/2004/wer7917/en/>\n\nBangladesh: Nipah Virus Outbreaks from Jan to Apr 2004\n----------------------------------------------\nOn 17 Jan 2004, a 9-year-old child from Kazipara village was brought to the\nemergency room of Goalanda health complex in Rajbari district, Bangladesh.\nThe patient had a 3-day history of fever and worsening neurological signs.\nHe died within 15 minutes of admission. Between 17 and 21 Jan 2004, 6\nadditional children (aged between 2 and 15 years) and a 28-year-old woman\n(mother of the youngest child) also died with the same symptoms. All\npatients came from the neighboring Kazipara and Juran Molla Para villages\nand lived in 2 groups of houses located within 1 km of each other. On 21\nJan 2004, Goalanda health authorities triggered the alert and sent a report\nto the central level. On 22 Jan 2004, a joint investigation mission with\nthe Institute of Epidemiology Disease Control and Research (IEDCR) of the\nMinistry of Health, the Centre for Health and Population Research (ICDDRB),\nand the WHO country office was dispatched to Goalanda to verify the rumors\nand to collect samples for laboratory diagnosis.\nBetween 22 and 27 Jan 2004, the investigation team was able to confirm the\noutbreak and to provide a 1st description of the epidemic. Blood samples\nobtained from several patients were sent to the Special Pathogen Branch\n(SPB) at the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC) in Atlanta,\nUSA. On 4 Feb 2004, SPB-CDC detected IgM antibodies against Nipah virus in\nthe serum of 5 patients. The laboratory investigations included ELISA tests\nfor IgG and IgM antibodies, reverse transcriptase polymerase chain reaction\nto detect the viral genome, and viral isolation. CDC was able to culture\nthe virus from 2 of the patients. Viral RNA was amplified to show that a\nNipah virus -- with about a 95 percent homology with that isolated during\nthe Malaysian outbreak in 1999 -- was the cause of the outbreak.\nSurveillance\n------------\nIn light of the findings of previous epidemic investigations and of the\nclinical presentation of the first 8 confirmed Bangladeshi cases, 3\ncategories were employed for case definition: (1) suspect, (2) probable,\nand (3) laboratory-confirmed. In addition, when samples were collected from\nprobable and suspect cases, they were reclassified as \"non-cases\" if they\nwere negative for Nipah virus IgM antibodies. Routine epidemiological\nsurveillance was reinforced by an active suspect-encephalitis\ncase-notification system -- implemented in Faridpur, Manikgonj, and Rajbari\ndistricts -- surrounding the outbreak epicenter. Surveillance was also\nreinforced by IEDCR across the entire territory.\nEpidemiology\n------------\nDescriptive epidemiology was undertaken based on the case definition. A\ncase of Nipah virus infection was any probable or laboratory-confirmed\ncase. As at 23 Feb 2004, 23 Nipah cases -- including 11\nlaboratory-confirmed cases -- had been identified in 6 districts (Faridpur,\nGolpagonj, Manikgonj, Joypurat, Naogaon, Rajbari). 17 of the 23 cases died\n(case-fatality rate of 74 percent). In all the affected districts, cases\nwere clustered geographically and in time. The 1st identified case\ndeveloped the 1st symptoms of the disease on 11 Jan 2004. Most of the\ndetected cases occurred between 12 and 25 Jan 2004 (for 20 out of 23 cases)\nsuggesting a common source of contamination. [Figure 1 (not shown here)\nillustrates the weekly distribution of cases (survivors and deaths) of\nNipah virus infection in 6 districts of Bangladesh as of 23 Feb 2004. -\nMod.CP.]\nThe main focus, with 12 Nipah cases, was identified in Goalanda, Rajbari\ndistrict, where an in-depth investigation was undertaken. Of the 12 cases,\n9 were male (75 percent) and aged under 19 years. The investigation failed\nto establish any link between these cases and sick pigs -- or other types\nof sick mammals -- as was observed during the Malaysian Nipah outbreak.\nConversely, it was noted in Goalanda that young boys collected and ate\nfruits in the trees before dawn. This observation gave rise to the\nhypothesis that contamination could have occurred while eating the same\nfruits that fruit bats (_Pteropodidae_) fed on during the night.\nSeveral epidemiological studies were conducted by the investigation team in\nGoalanda. These included a case-control study to identify risk factors for\ninfection, a seroprevalence study to assess the spread of the infection\n(antibody detection) in the general population, and a follow-up of\nconvalescent cases to assess antibody kinetics and duration of viral\nshedding. Fieldwork has been completed, and data analysis has begun.\nClinical description\n---------------------\nMost of the identified cases were transferred to the Dhaka Medical College\nHospital and the Dhaka Shishu Hospital where definitive case management was\norganized. Among 11 patients with laboratory-confirmed Nipah virus\ninfection, the most commonly reported signs and symptoms included fever\n(11/11), headache (4/11), vomiting (5/11), dizziness (5/11), altered\nconsciousness or total or partial loss of consciousness (11/11), coma\n(9/11), focal neurological signs (3/11), disorientation/hallucination\n(4/11), seizure (8/11), respiratory distress (6/11) and cough/cold (3/11).\nEcological studies\n------------------\nEcological studies were conducted to establish the source of human\ninfection and identify the natural reservoir of Nipah virus in Bangladesh.\nThe team focused its work in the Goalanda area and sampled domestic,\nperidomestic and wild animals living in or around the affected villages. It\nprioritized taxa associated with previous Nipah virus outbreaks in Malaysia\n(i.e. Megachiropteran bats and pigs). Laboratory investigations are\ncurrently under way at SPB-CDC, although preliminary results confirm that\nseveral _Pteropus_ flying foxes have evidence of Nipah virus infection.\nSocial mobilization and health education\n------------------\nThe Ministry of Health and the WHO team relayed all relevant information to\nthe press on a regular basis thereby keeping the public regularly informed\nabout the evolution of the situation and of investigations. Given the\nresults of the epidemiological investigation, it was proposed that several\nhealth education messages be prepared to improve hygiene while eating\nfruits (washing, peeling, cooking fruits before eating them, and\nhand-washing at home) in order to break the primary infection and\ntransmission of the disease. The support of medical anthropologists and\nexperts in social mobilization will be needed to prepare these messages.\nCluster of Nipah Cases in Faridpur District, Mar - Apr 2004\n-----------------\nOn 19 Apr 2004, the Bangladesh Ministry of Health reported one cluster of\n30 Nipah cases, including 18 deaths from 13 Mar to 14 Apr in Faridpur\ndistrict. Laboratory testing, performed by CDC, confirmed Nipah virus\ninfection in 16 of the cases.\nThe outbreak was investigated by a team comprising experts from the IEDCR,\nICDDRB and the WHO country office. All cases lived in Guha Laksmipur\nvillage or were in contact with a spiritual leader living in this village\nwho was himself a case. Direct contact with ill patients is suspected to\nhave played a role in the transmission of the disease and the spread of\nthis outbreak.\nThe team organized infection-control and safe clinical-management workshops\nfor local medical staff in different health care settings of the Faridpur\ndistrict. Investigations are continuing.\nConclusion\n----------\nAfter Meherpur (2001) and Naogaon (2003), Bangladesh faces again an\nepidemic of Nipah. As in 2001 and 2003, few cases were identified, but this\ntime suspect cases are more widely spread throughout the country. Until\nstudies have been completed, and control and prevention measures discussed,\nthe investigation team has proposed the following short-term recommendations:\n1. Creation of a task force for Nipah outbreaks using a multidisciplinary\napproach.\n2. Creation of special funds for outbreak response and to support families\nand hospitals.\n3. Institution of urgent measures to prevent nosocomial infection in all\nhospitals of the country (patient isolation, injection safety, safe waste\ndisposal, etc.).\n4. Preparation of prevention messages recommending hand-washing after\ntaking care of sick relatives and washing fruit, and peeling or cooking it,\nbefore eating.\nRecommendations also included medium-term activities such as strengthening\noutbreak detection and investigation capacity, strengthening basic\nlaboratory support, transferring Nipah laboratory diagnosis capacity to\nBangladesh, and conducting further studies on the ecology of _Pteropodidae_\nin Bangladesh.\nAn international workshop focusing on case-management, and a scientific\nmeeting on Nipah virus and Hendra virus, are planned to be held in Dhaka in\n2004 in collaboration with experts from Australia, Malaysia, Thailand and\nUnited States.\n--\nProMED-mail\n<promed@promedmail.org>\nSee Also\nNipah/Hendra-like virus - Bangladesh (2001/2003) 20040202.0398\nNipah/Hendra - like virus - Bangladesh (Rajbari) 20040206.0431\nNipah virus - Bangladesh (Faridpur) confirmed 20040420.1102\nUnexplained deaths - Bangladesh (Faridpur)(02): Nipah susp 20040419.1087\nUnexplained deaths - Bangladesh (Faridpur): RFI 20040407.0941\nUndiagnosed deaths - Bangladesh (Rajbari):RFI 20040125.0292\nUndiagnosed deaths - Bangladesh (Rajbari)(02): RFI 20040129.0336\nUndiagnosed deaths - Bangladesh (Rajbari) (03) 20040130.0346\nUndiagnosed deaths - Bangladesh (Rajbari)(04): Nip... 20040201.0387\n2002\n----\nNipah-like virus - Bangladesh (2001) 20020830.5187\n2001\n----\nUndiagnosed disease - Bangladesh: alert 20010524.1006\nUndiagnosed disease - Bangladesh: RFI 20010512.0919\nUnexplained deaths - India (North Bengal): RFI 20010220.0328\nUnexplained deaths - India (North Bengal) (11) 20010330.0643\n....................cp/msp/pg/mpp\n\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. Cosgriff <mcosgriff@hotmail.com>\nSource: The Star Online, 17 Jul 2000 [edited\nFour workers to undergo 'Nipah' test\n------------------------------------\n\nFour people here believed to be infected with the Nipah virus will be\ntransferred to the Sarawak Hospital in Kuching for a thorough medical\nexamination. State Assistant Minister for Public Health, Dr Soon Choon Teck,\nsaid the two farm workers and two abattoir workers had shown symptoms of the\ndisease. According to the State Health Department the four had developed\nantibody against the virus after they were infected. Dr Soon, who visited\nthem at the hospital here on Saturday, dismissed rumours that one of them\nwas suffering mental distress. He said they were worried that their families\nmight face financial difficulties, adding that the Welfare Department was\nlooking into ways to help them.\n\nState Veterinary authorities have decided to cull some 600 pigs from another\nfarm at KM18, Jalan Oya, as some of the animals there were reported to have\nfallen sick. A similar exercise to destroy more than 4500 animals from two\nother pig farms owned by the same operator [at Jalan Oya entered its fourth\nday yesterday. The farms were among 15 which were placed under quarantine\nfor suspected Nipah virus. Tests conducted in Australia from the blood\nsamples were found to be negative and the government lifted the quarantine\norder for eight of the farms. Decision on the remaining farms is expected to\nbe made after the results from the tests conducted on their owners, workers\nand family members are known.\n*********\n[2\nDate: Wed, 10 May 2000 04:01:53 -0700\nFrom: T. Wong <twong@pc.jaring.my>\nFurther comment on the current situation in Sarawak\n---------------------------------------------------\nThe current situation in Sarawak is that three farms with over 5000 pigs in\nSibu have been culled due to \"exposure\" to the Nipah virus. The authorities\napparently [have adopted the following main criteria (among others) to\nrequire culling of affected farms: (1) Samples of pig serum testing\npositive to both ELISA (done in the Veterinary Research Institute (VRI) and\nSNT tests (carried out in Australia); (2) Farm workers or owners living\nin/near farms detected with positive IgM titres. Any of the above\nconditions would result in the order to cull with financial relief provided\nby the government in line with those provided in Peninsular Malaysia.\nFor the farming community, the situation has [become confused by the fact\nthat samples considered positive by ELISA test in Malaysia have been scored\nnegative by SNT in Australia. Whether there is any problem in the\nprocessing or handling of the samples prior to despatch to Australia is\nsomething no one wants or is ready to answer. How good are the ELISA tests\ndone by UNIMAS and VRI? Although these tests are undertaken [according to\nthe guidelines of CDC and CSIRO, have they been properly validated? How\nspecific and sensitive are the ELISA (IgM or IgG) tests used for screening?\nAre there any publised report or authorities that can be cited? Although\nacceptable for surveillance or monitoring, can ELISA tests (or serial\npositive ELISA tests) be used as the basis or culling? The VRI is carrying\nout PCR and immunoperoxidase tests on samples obtained from some of the\nfarms in Sarawak. Early indications are that viral antigens similar to\nNipah virus has been identified in samples obtained from Sibu farms. Up to\nthis moment no clinical signs has yet been seen in the pigs in farms where\nthe initial ELISA tests were positive and where workers/owners tested\npositive with IgM titres. Should the pigs in farms in this category be\nculled? Should we wait until we are sure that the \"viral agent\" does cause\nclinical disease in pigs and human? It is understandable that the\nauthorities should choose to err on the side of caution and in the overall\ninterest of human health. However,the impact of [such decisions, if the\npolicies of the Federal government are followed, are not only going to\ncause great financial hardship and emotional distress in the pig farming\ncommunity, but they may be a blow from which the pig industry here may\nnever recover. Comments, proposals and directions are welcome by a farming\ncommunity under great stress.\n\n--\nT. State Assistant Minister for Public Health, Dr Soon Choon Teck,\nsaid the two farm workers and two abattoir workers had shown symptoms of the\ndisease. According to the State Health Department the four had developed\nantibody against the virus after they were infected. Dr Soon, who visited\nthem at the hospital here on Saturday, dismissed rumours that one of them\nwas suffering mental distress. He said they were worried that their families\nmight face financial difficulties, adding that the Welfare Department was\nlooking into ways to help them.\n\nState Veterinary authorities have decided to cull some 600 pigs from another\nfarm at KM18, Jalan Oya, as some of the animals there were reported to have\nfallen sick. A similar exercise to destroy more than 4500 animals from two\nother pig farms owned by the same operator [at Jalan Oya entered its fourth\nday yesterday. The farms were among 15 which were placed under quarantine\nfor suspected Nipah virus. Tests conducted in Australia from the blood\nsamples were found to be negative and the government lifted the quarantine\norder for eight of the farms. Decision on the remaining farms is expected to\nbe made after the results from the tests conducted on their owners, workers\nand family members are known.\n*********\n[2\nDate: Wed, 10 May 2000 04:01:53 -0700\nFrom: T. Wong <twong@pc.jaring.my>\nFurther comment on the current situation in Sarawak\n---------------------------------------------------\nThe current situation in Sarawak is that three farms with over 5000 pigs in\nSibu have been culled due to \"exposure\" to the Nipah virus. The authorities\napparently [have adopted the following main criteria (among others) to\nrequire culling of affected farms: (1) Samples of pig serum testing\npositive to both ELISA (done in the Veterinary Research Institute (VRI) and\nSNT tests (carried out in Australia); (2) Farm workers or owners living\nin/near farms detected with positive IgM titres. Any of the above\nconditions would result in the order to cull with financial relief provided\nby the government in line with those provided in Peninsular Malaysia.\nFor the farming community, the situation has [become confused by the fact\nthat samples considered positive by ELISA test in Malaysia have been scored\nnegative by SNT in Australia. Whether there is any problem in the\nprocessing or handling of the samples prior to despatch to Australia is\nsomething no one wants or is ready to answer. How good are the ELISA tests\ndone by UNIMAS and VRI? Although these tests are undertaken [according to\nthe guidelines of CDC and CSIRO, have they been properly validated? How\nspecific and sensitive are the ELISA (IgM or IgG) tests used for screening?\nAre there any publised report or authorities that can be cited? Although\nacceptable for surveillance or monitoring, can ELISA tests (or serial\npositive ELISA tests) be used as the basis or culling? The VRI is carrying\nout PCR and immunoperoxidase tests on samples obtained from some of the\nfarms in Sarawak. Early indications are that viral antigens similar to\nNipah virus has been identified in samples obtained from Sibu farms. Up to\nthis moment no clinical signs has yet been seen in the pigs in farms where\nthe initial ELISA tests were positive and where workers/owners tested\npositive with IgM titres. Should the pigs in farms in this category be\nculled? Should we wait until we are sure that the \"viral agent\" does cause\nclinical disease in pigs and human? It is understandable that the\nauthorities should choose to err on the side of caution and in the overall\ninterest of human health. However,the impact of [such decisions, if the\npolicies of the Federal government are followed, are not only going to\ncause great financial hardship and emotional distress in the pig farming\ncommunity, but they may be a blow from which the pig industry here may\nnever recover. Comments, proposals and directions are welcome by a farming\ncommunity under great stress.\n\n--\nT. Cosgriff <mcosgriff@hotmail.com>\nSource: The Star Online, 17 Jul 2000 [edited\nFour workers to undergo 'Nipah' test\n------------------------------------\n\nFour people here believed to be infected with the Nipah virus will be\ntransferred to the Sarawak Hospital in Kuching for a thorough medical\nexamination. State Assistant Minister for Public Health, Dr Soon Choon Teck,\nsaid the two farm workers and two abattoir workers had shown symptoms of the\ndisease. According to the State Health Department the four had developed\nantibody against the virus after they were infected. Dr Soon, who visited\nthem at the hospital here on Saturday, dismissed rumours that one of them\nwas suffering mental distress. He said they were worried that their families\nmight face financial difficulties, adding that the Welfare Department was\nlooking into ways to help them.\n\nState Veterinary authorities have decided to cull some 600 pigs from another\nfarm at KM18, Jalan Oya, as some of the animals there were reported to have\nfallen sick. A similar exercise to destroy more than 4500 animals from two\nother pig farms owned by the same operator [at Jalan Oya entered its fourth\nday yesterday. The farms were among 15 which were placed under quarantine\nfor suspected Nipah virus. Tests conducted in Australia from the blood\nsamples were found to be negative and the government lifted the quarantine\norder for eight of the farms. Decision on the remaining farms is expected to\nbe made after the results from the tests conducted on their owners, workers\nand family members are known.\n*********\n[2\nDate: Wed, 10 May 2000 04:01:53 -0700\nFrom: T. Wong <twong@pc.jaring.my>\nFurther comment on the current situation in Sarawak\n---------------------------------------------------\nThe current situation in Sarawak is that three farms with over 5000 pigs in\nSibu have been culled due to \"exposure\" to the Nipah virus. The authorities\napparently [have adopted the following main criteria (among others) to\nrequire culling of affected farms: (1) Samples of pig serum testing\npositive to both ELISA (done in the Veterinary Research Institute (VRI) and\nSNT tests (carried out in Australia); (2) Farm workers or owners living\nin/near farms detected with positive IgM titres. Any of the above\nconditions would result in the order to cull with financial relief provided\nby the government in line with those provided in Peninsular Malaysia.\nFor the farming community, the situation has [become confused by the fact\nthat samples considered positive by ELISA test in Malaysia have been scored\nnegative by SNT in Australia. Whether there is any problem in the\nprocessing or handling of the samples prior to despatch to Australia is\nsomething no one wants or is ready to answer. How good are the ELISA tests\ndone by UNIMAS and VRI? Although these tests are undertaken [according to\nthe guidelines of CDC and CSIRO, have they been properly validated? How\nspecific and sensitive are the ELISA (IgM or IgG) tests used for screening?\nAre there any publised report or authorities that can be cited? Although\nacceptable for surveillance or monitoring, can ELISA tests (or serial\npositive ELISA tests) be used as the basis or culling? The VRI is carrying\nout PCR and immunoperoxidase tests on samples obtained from some of the\nfarms in Sarawak. Early indications are that viral antigens similar to\nNipah virus has been identified in samples obtained from Sibu farms. Up to\nthis moment no clinical signs has yet been seen in the pigs in farms where\nthe initial ELISA tests were positive and where workers/owners tested\npositive with IgM titres. Should the pigs in farms in this category be\nculled? Should we wait until we are sure that the \"viral agent\" does cause\nclinical disease in pigs and human? It is understandable that the\nauthorities should choose to err on the side of caution and in the overall\ninterest of human health. However,the impact of [such decisions, if the\npolicies of the Federal government are followed, are not only going to\ncause great financial hardship and emotional distress in the pig farming\ncommunity, but they may be a blow from which the pig industry here may\nnever recover. Comments, proposals and directions are welcome by a farming\ncommunity under great stress.\n\n--\nT. This clearly is not a new case, just the sad end\nof a long clinical history. The previously reported fatal case from\nSelangor died the end of January.\nThere is nothing new here, except that illness caused by Nipah virus can\ncause death remote from the acute illness. According to this article,\npeople in Malaysia with antibody to Nipah virus are concerned that they\nmight become ill sometime from now. That is unlikely. What is likely is\nthat those are the only (about 200) people in Malaysia who are protected\nagainst Nipah virus infection.\nThe concern, of course, is that humans (and pigs) can be persistently and\nasymptomatically infected and then show signs of illness many months after\nprimary infection. There is no evidence for that at this time.\n--\nCharles H. This clearly is not a new case, just the sad end\nof a long clinical history. The previously reported fatal case from\nSelangor died the end of January.\nThere is nothing new here, except that illness caused by Nipah virus can\ncause death remote from the acute illness. According to this article,\npeople in Malaysia with antibody to Nipah virus are concerned that they\nmight become ill sometime from now. That is unlikely. There is no evidence for that at this time.\n--\nCharles H. - Malaysia: RFI 990319071030\nHendra-like virus? - Malaysia (02) 990319084147\nHendra-like virus - Malaysia (21) 990408163833\nDate: Sat, 31 Jul 1999\nFrom: Chan Yow Cheong mailto:chanyowcheong@pacific.net.sg\nProMED-mail Regional Moderator for Asia\nSource: Lam Sai Kit, Kenneth mailto:lamsk@medicine.med.um.edu.my and M\nVadivale SMTP:vale@pop.jaring.my, 30 Jul 1999 [edited\n\n[Abstract of scientific paper presented at a Nipah Virus Encephalitis\nseminar held on 26 July 1999 at the University of Malaya Medical Centre\n(UMMC) in Malaysia. - Mod.CYC\nNipah Virus Encephalitis : Tracking A Killer Virus\n--------------------------------------------------\nChua KB, Lam SK\nDepartment of Medical Microbiology\nThe outbreak of viral encephalitis started in Perak in late September 1998\nand spread to Negeri Sembilan by late February 1999. We received\ncerebrospinal fluid specimens from Seremban Hospital on 1st March and\nworked on a fast track to isolate and identify the killer virus.\nAlthough we confirmed several cases of Japanese encephalitis infection\nserologically, epidemiological evidence suggested the existence of another\nvirus. Specimens were inoculated into a variety of host systems and within\nfive days, we detected a virus which caused syncytial formation in Vero\ncells. Electron microscopy revealed pleomorphic viral particles ranging in\nsize from 160 - 300 nm, making us suspect that it is a member of the\nparamyxovirus family.\nAt CDC, Fort Collins, arborviruses were ruled out and electron microscopy\nconfirmed our own observation. Further studies at CDC, Atlanta, revealed\nthat the virus was related antigenically to the Australian Hendra virus.\nSince it is obviously a new virus, it was named Nipah virus after the\nlocality of the outbreak.\nAfter confirming that Nipah virus is a paramyxovirus, we advised the\nMinistry of Health on relevant control measures, which included the\navoidance of direct contact with infected pigs and the liberal use of\ndisinfectants in pig farms. Based on our suggestion, ribavarin was used in\nthe management of infected patients showing early symptoms. We also\nestablished that throat gargles and urine from patients contained the virus\nand the information was used to prevent nosocomial infections.\nTracking this killer virus, from isolation to identification, took exactly\n17 days.\n--\nProMED-mail\ne-mail: promed@usa.healthnet.org\n......................................jw/es\n--\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. - Malaysia: RFI 990319071030\nHendra-like virus? - Malaysia (02) 990319084147\nHendra-like virus - Malaysia (21) 990408163833\nDate: Sat, 31 Jul 1999\nFrom: Chan Yow Cheong mailto:chanyowcheong@pacific.net.sg\nProMED-mail Regional Moderator for Asia\nSource: Lam Sai Kit, Kenneth mailto:lamsk@medicine.med.um.edu.my and M\nVadivale SMTP:vale@pop.jaring.my, 30 Jul 1999 [edited\n\n[Abstract of scientific paper presented at a Nipah Virus Encephalitis\nseminar held on 26 July 1999 at the University of Malaya Medical Centre\n(UMMC) in Malaysia. - Mod.CYC\nNipah Virus Encephalitis : Tracking A Killer Virus\n--------------------------------------------------\nChua KB, Lam SK\nDepartment of Medical Microbiology\nThe outbreak of viral encephalitis started in Perak in late September 1998\nand spread to Negeri Sembilan by late February 1999. We received\ncerebrospinal fluid specimens from Seremban Hospital on 1st March and\nworked on a fast track to isolate and identify the killer virus.\nAlthough we confirmed several cases of Japanese encephalitis infection\nserologically, epidemiological evidence suggested the existence of another\nvirus. Specimens were inoculated into a variety of host systems and within\nfive days, we detected a virus which caused syncytial formation in Vero\ncells. Electron microscopy revealed pleomorphic viral particles ranging in\nsize from 160 - 300 nm, making us suspect that it is a member of the\nparamyxovirus family.\nAt CDC, Fort Collins, arborviruses were ruled out and electron microscopy\nconfirmed our own observation. Further studies at CDC, Atlanta, revealed\nthat the virus was related antigenically to the Australian Hendra virus.\nSince it is obviously a new virus, it was named Nipah virus after the\nlocality of the outbreak.\nAfter confirming that Nipah virus is a paramyxovirus, we advised the\nMinistry of Health on relevant control measures, which included the\navoidance of direct contact with infected pigs and the liberal use of\ndisinfectants in pig farms. Based on our suggestion, ribavarin was used in\nthe management of infected patients showing early symptoms. We also\nestablished that throat gargles and urine from patients contained the virus\nand the information was used to prevent nosocomial infections.\nTracking this killer virus, from isolation to identification, took exactly\n17 days.\n--\nProMED-mail\ne-mail: promed@usa.healthnet.org\n......................................jw/es\n--\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed. The report,\nfilmed in Malaysia, included much footage from infected properties\nincluding the first infected property. The program also claimed, I think on\nthe basis of retrospective serology, that the first human cases were\nactually in November 1997. This is a synopsis of the program extracted\nfrom their website, i.e., their words not mine.\n*****\n\"A lot of people could have been saved if we had not ignored some rather\nuncomfortable facts\", [said] Dr Henry Too Hing Lee, Animal Diseases\nSpecialist, Malaysia.\n\"We see this as another instance of a worldwide trend where diseases\npreviously unknown to medical and veterinary science are suddenly\nemerging\", [said] Dr Peter Daniels, Veterinary Epidemiologist, CSIRO.\nOne in three families lost a loved one when a strange disease swept through\nthe Malaysian village of Bukit Pelandok and claimed 80 lives. But the\ndeadly Nipah virus should never have made it to this prosperous pig farming\ncommunity.\nThis week Foreign Correspondent presents dramatic new evidence that the\naggressive, species hopping virus surfaced long before authorities acted\nand far from Bukit Pelandok, now a quarantined ghost town.\nInvasive farming practices set Malaysia on a collision course with the\nkiller disease as political inaction and ineptitude saw the virus spread.\nThe advice given out by the Malaysian Government \"\u0085 was definitely fatal\nfor a lot of pig farmers\", [said] Dr Henry Too Hing Lee, an animal diseases\nspecialist at University Putra, Malaysia.\nTim Lester's extensive investigation of the world's latest virulent\ndisaster features extensive material recorded by Malaysia's Veterinary\nDepartment of the belated campaign to stem the outbreak. There are also\nsombre warnings from experts on the ground, including CSIRO Veterinary\nEpidemiologist Dr Peter Daniels that deadly viruses, previously unknown to\nscience, are on the march around the world where new aggressive farming\npractices are forging into ancient and untouched landscapes.\nTapes are available. The report,\nfilmed in Malaysia, included much footage from infected properties\nincluding the first infected property. The program also claimed, I think on\nthe basis of retrospective serology, that the first human cases were\nactually in November 1997. This is a synopsis of the program extracted\nfrom their website, i.e., their words not mine.\n*****\n\"A lot of people could have been saved if we had not ignored some rather\nuncomfortable facts\", [said] Dr Henry Too Hing Lee, Animal Diseases\nSpecialist, Malaysia.\n\"We see this as another instance of a worldwide trend where diseases\npreviously unknown to medical and veterinary science are suddenly\nemerging\", [said] Dr Peter Daniels, Veterinary Epidemiologist, CSIRO.\nOne in three families lost a loved one when a strange disease swept through\nthe Malaysian village of Bukit Pelandok and claimed 80 lives. But the\ndeadly Nipah virus should never have made it to this prosperous pig farming\ncommunity.\nThis week Foreign Correspondent presents dramatic new evidence that the\naggressive, species hopping virus surfaced long before authorities acted\nand far from Bukit Pelandok, now a quarantined ghost town.\nInvasive farming practices set Malaysia on a collision course with the\nkiller disease as political inaction and ineptitude saw the virus spread.\nThe advice given out by the Malaysian Government \"\u0085 was definitely fatal\nfor a lot of pig farmers\", [said] Dr Henry Too Hing Lee, an animal diseases\nspecialist at University Putra, Malaysia.\nTim Lester's extensive investigation of the world's latest virulent\ndisaster features extensive material recorded by Malaysia's Veterinary\nDepartment of the belated campaign to stem the outbreak. 990415153739\nNipah virus - Malaysia (10) 990428233320\nNipah virus (Hendra-like), natural history 990412220834\nNipah virus, natural history 990416162248\nDate: Sat, 1 May 1999\nFrom: Chan Yow Cheong mailto:chanyowcheong@pacific.net.sg\nProMED-mail Regional Moderator for Asia\nSource: Bernama, 1 May 1999 [edited\n\nThe Health Ministry has formed primary teams to carry out extensive medical\nfollow-ups on viral encephalitis (VE) patients who had been discharged,\nespecially those formerly infected with the Nipah or Hendra-like virus.\nIts minister Datuk Chua Jui Meng said the teams would only be available at\nhospitals with VE patients and would be headed by the chief physician or\nthe chief neurologist wherever available. \"This is the first time we are\nfacing the Nipah virus and we need to have as much information such as the\nclinical features of the disease, we want to know what happens during the\nacute phase of the patients' illness and after their discharge,\" he said.\nIt would also help the doctors and rehabilitation teams to monitor the\npatients' recovery, late complications and possible reactivation of the\nviruses, Chua told reporters.\nThe follow-up exercise would be carried out for one year with the first\nfive follow-ups to be done on a monthly basis while the sixth follow-up\nwould be done on the ninth month. \"The seventh follow-up will be done 12\nmonths after discharge,\" said Chua, adding that patients would be assessed\non their general health, functional status, whether there were damaged or\ndysfunctional organs as well as their psychological well-being.\nChua said the ministry knew the danger posed by the Hendra virus [in\nAustralia based on an incident involving a patient who died 13 months\nafter being discharged from hospital.\nThe minister also said the cabinet, at its weekly meeting last week, had\ndecided to set up a permanent council for zoonotic diseases but declined to\nreveal more details when asked.\nIn a related development, Chua denied a local newspaper report today which\nsaid that a pig farm worker at Seelong, 20 kilometres from Johor Bahru [in\nthe southernmost Johor state, fell into a coma and was now in the\nintensive care unit of the Sultanah Aminah Hospital. "main_text": "NIPAH VIRUS - MALAYSIA (11)\n***************************\nA ProMED-mail post\n<http://www.healthnet.org/programs/promed.html>\nSee Also\nNipah virus - Malaysia 990415153739\nNipah virus - Malaysia (10) 990428233320\nNipah virus (Hendra-like), natural history 990412220834\nNipah virus, natural history 990416162248\nDate: Sat, 1 May 1999\nFrom: Chan Yow Cheong mailto:chanyowcheong@pacific.net.sg\nProMED-mail Regional Moderator for Asia\nSource: Bernama, 1 May 1999 [edited\n\nThe Health Ministry has formed primary teams to carry out extensive medical\nfollow-ups on viral encephalitis (VE) patients who had been discharged,\nespecially those formerly infected with the Nipah or Hendra-like virus.\nIts minister Datuk Chua Jui Meng said the teams would only be available at\nhospitals with VE patients and would be headed by the chief physician or\nthe chief neurologist wherever available. \"This is the first time we are\nfacing the Nipah virus and we need to have as much information such as the\nclinical features of the disease, we want to know what happens during the\nacute phase of the patients' illness and after their discharge,\" he said.\nIt would also help the doctors and rehabilitation teams to monitor the\npatients' recovery, late complications and possible reactivation of the\nviruses, Chua told reporters.\nThe follow-up exercise would be carried out for one year with the first\nfive follow-ups to be done on a monthly basis while the sixth follow-up\nwould be done on the ninth month. \"The seventh follow-up will be done 12\nmonths after discharge,\" said Chua, adding that patients would be assessed\non their general health, functional status, whether there were damaged or\ndysfunctional organs as well as their psychological well-being.\nChua said the ministry knew the danger posed by the Hendra virus [in\nAustralia based on an incident involving a patient who died 13 months\nafter being discharged from hospital.\nThe minister also said the cabinet, at its weekly meeting last week, had\ndecided to set up a permanent council for zoonotic diseases but declined to\nreveal more details when asked.\nIn a related development, Chua denied a local newspaper report today which\nsaid that a pig farm worker at Seelong, 20 kilometres from Johor Bahru [in\nthe southernmost Johor state, fell into a coma and was now in the\nintensive care unit of the Sultanah Aminah Hospital. Chua said he had\nchecked and found no such case existed and wanted the story which appeared\non the front page of the newspaper to be corrected immediately as it would\ncause undue alarm among the public.\n--\nProMED-mail\ne-mail: promed@usa.healthnet.org\n...........................................es\n--\n*##########################################################*\nProMED-mail makes every effort to verify the reports that\nare posted, but the accuracy and completeness of the\ninformation, and of any statements or opinions based\nthereon, are not guaranteed.
